Defending the Proof: The Counterexample of God's Actions
Counterexample and its answer regarding divine action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the counterexample to the fourth proof (that it applies to God's actions too) and the answer based on the nature of divine will.

Core Idea
God's actions are voluntary because His will is eternal and not preceded by knowledge.

The fourth proof seems to imply that any being whose actions are known by God would be compelled. But God's own actions are also known by Him—does that mean God is compelled? The passage raises this counterexample and provides a sophisticated answer distinguishing between divine and human will. It explains that a voluntary act is one the agent can leave at the moment of willing, and God's will, being eternal, could have been otherwise, so no compulsion arises.

Guided Path
1
Read the counterexample
Start with the counterexample: the same reasoning would apply to God's actions, implying they are compelled too. But this is rejected by consensus.
This is a common objection to the proof.
2
Examine the answer
The answer defines the voluntary as that which the agent can leave at the time of willing, not after. God's will is eternal, so at the moment of willing (in pre‑eternity), He could have willed otherwise.
This avoids compulsion because there is no prior knowledge forcing the will.
3
Understand the role of knowledge and will together
The passage states that since God's will is attached in pre‑eternity without prior knowledge, there is no necessity. Knowledge and will together cause no problem, unlike the human case.
This preserves divine freedom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (الرابع) الدليل الرابع : معلوم الله تعالى من فعل العبد . أما وقوعه فيجب أو لا وقوعه فيمتنع، فلا يبقى في مكنة العبد، وإن كان ممكناً في نفسه . فإن قيل : المعلوم وقوعه بقدرة العبد واختياره . قلنا : فيجب ذلك ويعود المحذور ونوقض بفعل الباري). قد ثبت أن الله تعالى عالم بالجزئيات (۱) ما كان وما سيكون، وأنه يستحيل عليه الجهل وكل ما علم الله أنه يقع يجب وقوعه، وكل ما علم الله أنه لا يقع يمتنع وقوعه، نظراً إلى تعلق العلم، وإن كان ممكناً في نفسه وبالنظر إلى ذاته، ولا شيء من الواجب والممتنع باقياً في مكنة العبد بمعنى أنه إن شاء فعله وإن شاء تركه. فإن قيل : يجوز أن يعلم الله تعالى أن فعل العبد يقع بقدرته واختياره، فلا يكون خارجاً عن مكنته . من قلنا : فيجب أن يقع البتة بقدرته واختياره بحيث لا يتمكن من اختيار الترك، وهذا هو المراد بالانتهاء إلى الاضطرار. غاية الأمر أن يكون بإيجاده لكن لا على وجه الاستقلال والاختيار التام كما هو مذهب المعتزلة. وقد أشرنا إلى أن القصد (٢) بعض الأدلة إلى الالزام دون الإتمام نعم يرد نقض الدليل بفعل الباري تعالى الجريانه فيه مع الاتفاق على كونه بقدرته واختياره ويمكن دفعه بأن الاختياري ما يكون الفاعل متمكناً من تركه عند إرادة فعله لا بعده، وهذا متحقق في فعل الباري، لأن إرادته قديمة متعلقة في الأزل بأنه يقع في وقته وجائز أن يتعلق حينئذ بتركه (۳) ، وليس حينئذ سابقة علم ليتحقق الوجوب، أو الامتناع إذ لا قبل للأزل، فالحاصل أن تعلق العلم والإرادة معاً فلا محذور بخلاف إرادة العبد، وتقرير الإمام في المطالب أنه لما وجب في الأزل وقوع الفعل أو لا وقوعه في وقته لزم أن يكون لهذا الوجوب سبب ، وليس من العبد لأن الحادث لا يصلح سبباً للأزلي . بل من الله تعالى . وليس هو العلم لأنه تابع للمعلوم، لا مستتبع بل القدرة والإرادة، إذ بهما التأثير فثبت أن المؤثر في فعل العبد قدرة الله تعالى إما ابتداء أو بوسط وهو المطلوب، وهذا ضعيف جداً، لكن النقض مندفع عنه. العالية (٤) هو
He said (the fourth): The fourth proof: What God knows of the servant's action: if its occurrence [is known], it is necessary; or its non-occurrence [is known], it is impossible. So nothing remains in the servant's ability, even if it is possible in itself. If it is said: The thing known to occur by the servant's power and choice. We say: Then that is necessary, and the same problem returns. And it is objected by the action of the Creator. It has been established that God the Exalted knows particulars (1), what was and what will be, and that ignorance is impossible for Him. And everything that God knows will occur must occur, and everything that God knows will not occur cannot occur, from the perspective of the attachment of knowledge, even if it is possible in itself and from the perspective of its essence. And nothing of the necessary and the impossible remains in the servant's ability in the sense that if he wills he does it and if he wills he leaves it. If it is said: It is permissible that God the Exalted knows that the servant's action will occur by his power and choice, so it is not outside his ability. From [that] we say: Then it must necessarily occur by his power and choice such that he is not able to choose leaving. And this is what is meant by the conclusion to compulsion. The only difference is that it is by His creation but not in the manner of independence and complete choice as is the doctrine of the Mu'tazilites. And we have indicated that the intent of some proofs is imposition (ilzam) not completion (itmam). Yes, a counterexample to the proof arises from the action of the Creator the Exalted, because it applies to it [the same reasoning] despite agreement that it is by His power and choice. And it can be repelled by [saying] that the voluntary is that of which the agent is able to leave at the time of willing the action, not after it. And this is realized in the action of the Creator, because His will is eternal, attached in pre-eternity that it will occur at its time, and it is possible that it be attached then to its leaving (3), and there is not then prior knowledge to necessitate necessity or impossibility, since there is no before for pre-eternity. So the result is that the attachment of knowledge and will together, so there is no problem, unlike the will of the servant. And the exposition of the Imam in al-Matalib [al-'Aliya] that when the occurrence or non-occurrence of the action at its time became necessary in pre-eternity, it follows that there is a cause for this necessity, and it is not from the servant because the temporal cannot be a cause for the eternal. Rather from God the Exalted. And it is not knowledge because it follows the known, not a necessitator, but rather power and will, for effect is by them. So it is established that the influencer in the servant's action is the power of God the Exalted, either directly or indirectly, and that is what is sought. And this is very weak, but the counterexample is repelled from it. al-ʿĀliya (4) it is.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof and its extensive defense. The proof argues that whatever God knows about a servant's action—whether it will occur or not—is necessary or impossible respectively, leaving no real power in the servant. An objection states that God may know the action will occur by the servant's power and choice, but the answer insists this still necessitates the action, leading to compulsion. A counterexample is raised: the same reasoning would apply to God's own actions, implying they also are compelled. The answer distinguishes that a voluntary act is one the agent can leave at the moment of willing, not after; God's will is eternal and could have been attached to leaving, so no compulsion. The passage then references al-Razi's argument in al-Matalib al-ʿAliya, which posits that the eternal necessity of occurrence requires a cause from God alone—power and will—not from the servant. However, the author notes this is very weak (da'if jiddan), though the counterexample is deflected.

Key Terms
نقض بفعل الباري
اختياري
إرادة قديمة
تعلق العلم
تعلق الإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the proof implies God's actions are also compelled, but the answer clarifies the difference.
Correction: God's actions are not compelled because His will is eternal and could have been otherwise without prior knowledge forcing it.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes God's actions from human actions in the context of the fourth proof?
A) God's actions are not known by Him
B) God's will is eternal and not preceded by knowledge
C) God cannot choose otherwise
D) The proof does not apply to God

Key Takeaway
The counterexample is answered by noting that divine will is eternal and not necessitated by prior knowledge, thus preserving divine freedom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face trials, do you ever feel that God's decree is a cold, mechanical chain of events? How does knowing that God's will is eternal and freely chosen change your trust in His wisdom?
?
You often attribute your own actions to your knowledge and desires. But have you ever considered that your knowledge often dictates your choices, while God's knowledge does not constrain His will? How does this affect your understanding of His mercy and justice?
?
This lesson shows that God's actions are voluntary because He could have willed otherwise. When you pray for something, do you truly believe that God could have chosen differently, and that His eternal will is a personal response to your need? Examine your heart: Do you pray with the conviction that your prayer can affect the eternal decree, or do you see it as a formality?

Al-Razi's Argument and Its Critique
Al-Razi's argument from al-Matalib and its critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's argument that the eternal necessity of an action requires a cause from God, not the servant, and the author's critique that this argument is weak.

Core Idea
Eternal necessity implies a divine cause, not human power.

After defending the proof from the counterexample, the passage presents a deeper analysis attributed to Imam al-Razi (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) from his work al-Matalib al-ʿAliya. He argues that since the occurrence or non‑occurrence of an action is necessary in pre‑eternity, there must be a cause for that necessity, and that cause cannot be the servant because the temporal cannot affect the eternal. The cause must be God's power and will. The author of the sharh, however, dismisses this argument as very weak (da'if jiddan), though he notes it still repels the counterexample.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Razi's argument
Focus on the reasoning: the eternal necessity of the action requires a cause, not from the servant but from God, specifically His power and will.
Al-Razi is trying to prove that God's power directly causes the servant's action.
2
Understand the critique
The author labels this argument as 'very weak' but does not elaborate why. He only says the counterexample is deflected.
This suggests the author finds the reasoning flawed but not the conclusion.
3
Reflect on the overall debate
Consider how this argument fits into the larger discussion on qadar. Al-Razi's argument is one way to prove divine control, but it is not accepted by all Ash'aris.
The sharh author's criticism indicates disagreement within the school.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Divine Decree

The Proof from the Impossibility of Recreating an Action
Al-Taftazani presents the first proof: if the servant could create his action, he could recreate it, which is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the early Ash'ari proof that if the servant could create his own action, he would be able to recreate it, which is impossible, thus proving the action is created by God.

Core Idea
The ability to create an action would imply the ability to recreate it, which no human can do.

Al-Taftazani presents the first of several proofs from early scholars to show that human actions are created by God alone. This proof argues from the impossibility of recreating an action. If the servant were the true originator, he could bring back an identical action at any time—but this is clearly not the case. The proof includes an objection and a response, which you will explore.

Guided Path
1
Read the first proof passage
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the conditional structure: if the servant were capable, then he would be capable of recreation.
The passage begins with 'للمتقدمين على كون فعل العبد بقدرة الله...'.
2
Understand the logical implication
The proof argues that the possibility of power over a thing does not change with time. Thus, if one can create initially, one can recreate later.
This is the 'wajh al-luzum'.
3
Analyze the objection and response
An objection is raised: recreation of a non-existent may be prevented by conditions. The response is that the opponent already admits the two premises, so the objection fails.
The objection and response show dialectical reasoning.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Since the consequent (ability to recreate) is false by consensus, the antecedent (servant's own power) must be false. Hence the action is by God's power.
This supports the Ash'ari position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من أدلة المتقدمين القائلين أن فعل العبد بقدرة الرب قال (وقد يستدل) بأنه لو قدر على فعله لقدر على إعادته على مثله وعلى خلق الجسم إذ لا مصحح الحدوث والامكان ولكان فعله كخلق الإيمان أحسن من فعل الباري كخلق سوی الشيطان، ولما صح سؤال الإيمان ولا الشكر عليه . للمتقدمين على كون فعل العبد بقدرة الله دون قدرته وجوه منها أن العبد لو كان قادراً على فعله إيجاداً واختراعاً لكان قادراً على إعادته، واللازم منتف إجماعاً وجه اللزوم أن إمكان القدرة منه يستلزم ماهيته لا يختلف باختلاف الأوقات، ولهذا يصح الاستدلال على قدرة الله تعالى على الإعادة بقدرته على الابتداء كما نطق به التنزيل احتجاجاً على منكري الإعادة بالنشأة الأولى. والاعتراض يمنع إمكان إعادة المعدوم مستنداً بأنه يجوز أن يكون خصوصية البدء شرطاً أو خصوصية العود مانعاً أو يمنع عدم قدرة العبد على الإعادة ليس بشيء لأن الخصم معترف بالمقدمتين. ومنها أنه لو كان قادراً على إيجاد فعله لكان قادراً على إيجاد مثله لأن حكم الأمثال واحد لكنا نقطع بأنه يتعذر علينا أن نفعل الآن مثل ما فعلناه سابقاً بلا تفاوت، وإن بذلنا الجهد في التدبر والاحتياط ، ومنها أنه لو كان قادراً على إيجاد فعله لكان قادراً على إيجاد كل ممكن من الأجسام والأعراض لأن المصحح للمقدورية هو الإمكان أو الحدوث، والمقدور هو إعطاء الوجود ولا تفاوت في شيء منها باعتراف الخصم ، ولا يرد النقض
Among the proofs of the early scholars who say that the action of the servant is by the power of the Lord: He said, 'And it is argued' that if he were capable of his action, he would be capable of its recreation upon its like and upon the creation of the body, since there is no corrector of origination and possibility, and his action would be like creating faith better than the action of the Creator like creating the form of Satan, and it would not be valid to ask for faith nor to thank him. The early scholars have for the position that the action of the servant is by God's power not by his own power aspects, among them: that if the servant were capable of his action, bringing into existence and inventing, he would be capable of recreating it, and the consequent is unanimously negated. The aspect of the implication is that the possibility of power from it necessitates its essence does not differ with difference of times, and for this it is valid to deduce God's power over recreation from His power over origination, as the revelation declared as an argument against deniers of recreation by the first creation. And the objection prevents the possibility of recreating the non-existent, based on the possibility that the specificity of the beginning is a condition or the specificity of return is an obstacle, or it prevents the non-ability of the servant to recreate is nothing because the opponent acknowledges the two premises. And among them: that if he were capable of bringing his action into existence, he would be capable of bringing its like into existence because the rule of similars is one, yet we are certain that it is impossible for us now to do the same as we did previously without difference, even if we exert effort in deliberation and precaution. And among them: that if he were capable of bringing his action into existence, he would be capable of bringing every possible thing into existence from bodies and accidents because the corrector of being an object of power is possibility or origination, and the object of power is bestowing existence, and there is no difference in any of them according to the opponent's admission, and the refutation does not hold.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage presents several proofs from early scholars (al-mutaqaddimin) to support the Ash'ari position that the servant's action is created by God's power, not by the servant's own power. The first proof is stated briefly: if the servant were capable of his action, he would be capable of recreating it, and also of creating a body (since there is no distinct cause for origination and possibility), and his action would be comparable to God's creation—but he cannot do such things. The text then elaborates: the early scholars give multiple 'wujuh' (aspects). The first aspect: if the servant were truly the originator (ijadan wa ikhtira'an) of his act, he would be able to recreate it; but this is impossible by consensus. The implication (wajh al-luzum) is that the possibility of power over a thing continues as long as the thing's essence is possible, and essence does not change with time. Thus, just as God's power to begin creation implies His power to recreate, so it would be for the servant. An objection is raised: the recreation of a non-existent thing may be prevented by specific conditions. The response dismisses this because the opponent already admits the two premises (that power over the first implies power over the second). The second proof: if one can create an action, one can create an exactly similar action (because the rule of similars is one), but we know empirically that we cannot repeat an action without any difference, no matter how hard we try. The third proof: if one could create his own action, he could create all possible things (bodies and accidents), because the ground for being an object of power is either possibility or origination, and there is no difference among possible things in this regard. Therefore, the claim that the servant has power over his action leads to absurd consequences. The text cuts off at 'wa la yarid al-naqd' (and the refutation does not hold) which likely continues.

Key Terms
فعل العبد
قدرة الرب
إعادة
إمكان القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Humans have the power to create their own actions independently.
Correction: This proof shows that if humans could create their actions, they would also be able to recreate them, which is impossible. Therefore, the true creator is God.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the claim that if the servant could create his action, he could recreate it?
A) Because the essence of the action does not change over time.
B) Because the servant has free will.
C) Because God gives him power for both.
D) Because the action is an accident.

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows that the servant cannot be the true creator of his action because that would imply the ability to recreate it, which is empirically and consensually false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you cannot recreate even a single word you just spoke, what does this reveal about your absolute dependence on God for every action? How does this truth change how you view your own achievements and failures? Will you begin each day acknowledging that your very movements are a gift from Him?
?
The proof forces you to admit that your actions are not truly your own creation. Yet you will be held accountable for them. Does this truth make you feel helpless or liberated? How can you live with both humility before God’s power and personal responsibility for your choices?
?
Notice that the objection in the proof is dismissed because the opponent already concedes the key premises. Are there areas in your faith where you accept the premises but resist the logical conclusion? Why do you resist, and what would it cost you to fully embrace the truth?

The Proof from the Impossibility of Creating an Exactly Similar Action
Al-Taftazani presents the second proof: if the servant could create his action, he could create its exact like, which is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof: that if the servant could create his action, he would be able to create an exactly similar action, but we know empirically that we cannot repeat an action without difference.

Core Idea
If you truly create your action, you should be able to create its exact copy—but you cannot.

The early scholars present a second proof based on the rule of similars. If a person were the originator of his action, he would be able to produce another action identical to it in all respects. Yet we know from experience that no matter how hard we try, we cannot repeat an action without any variation. This shows that our actions are not under our own creative power.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof passage
Focus on the Arabic and translation for the second proof: 'ومنها أنه لو كان قادراً على إيجاد فعله لكان قادراً على إيجاد مثله'.
This is a separate 'wajh' from the first.
2
Understand the rule of similars
The argument uses the principle 'hukm al-amthal wahid'—the rule of similars is one. If two things are exactly alike, what holds for one holds for the other.
So if you can create one, you can create its like.
3
Consider the empirical evidence
We know from experience that we cannot perform the same action twice without any difference, even with maximum effort.
This is the basis for negating the consequent.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since the consequent (ability to create a like) is false, the antecedent (servant's own creation) must be false.
Thus, the action is created by God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من أدلة المتقدمين القائلين أن فعل العبد بقدرة الرب قال (وقد يستدل) بأنه لو قدر على فعله لقدر على إعادته على مثله وعلى خلق الجسم إذ لا مصحح الحدوث والامكان ولكان فعله كخلق الإيمان أحسن من فعل الباري كخلق سوی الشيطان، ولما صح سؤال الإيمان ولا الشكر عليه . للمتقدمين على كون فعل العبد بقدرة الله دون قدرته وجوه منها أن العبد لو كان قادراً على فعله إيجاداً واختراعاً لكان قادراً على إعادته، واللازم منتف إجماعاً وجه اللزوم أن إمكان القدرة منه يستلزم ماهيته لا يختلف باختلاف الأوقات، ولهذا يصح الاستدلال على قدرة الله تعالى على الإعادة بقدرته على الابتداء كما نطق به التنزيل احتجاجاً على منكري الإعادة بالنشأة الأولى. والاعتراض يمنع إمكان إعادة المعدوم مستنداً بأنه يجوز أن يكون خصوصية البدء شرطاً أو خصوصية العود مانعاً أو يمنع عدم قدرة العبد على الإعادة ليس بشيء لأن الخصم معترف بالمقدمتين. ومنها أنه لو كان قادراً على إيجاد فعله لكان قادراً على إيجاد مثله لأن حكم الأمثال واحد لكنا نقطع بأنه يتعذر علينا أن نفعل الآن مثل ما فعلناه سابقاً بلا تفاوت، وإن بذلنا الجهد في التدبر والاحتياط ، ومنها أنه لو كان قادراً على إيجاد فعله لكان قادراً على إيجاد كل ممكن من الأجسام والأعراض لأن المصحح للمقدورية هو الإمكان أو الحدوث، والمقدور هو إعطاء الوجود ولا تفاوت في شيء منها باعتراف الخصم ، ولا يرد النقض
Among the proofs of the early scholars who say that the action of the servant is by the power of the Lord: He said, 'And it is argued' that if he were capable of his action, he would be capable of its recreation upon its like and upon the creation of the body, since there is no corrector of origination and possibility, and his action would be like creating faith better than the action of the Creator like creating the form of Satan, and it would not be valid to ask for faith nor to thank him. The early scholars have for the position that the action of the servant is by God's power not by his own power aspects, among them: that if the servant were capable of his action, bringing into existence and inventing, he would be capable of recreating it, and the consequent is unanimously negated. The aspect of the implication is that the possibility of power from it necessitates its essence does not differ with difference of times, and for this it is valid to deduce God's power over recreation from His power over origination, as the revelation declared as an argument against deniers of recreation by the first creation. And the objection prevents the possibility of recreating the non-existent, based on the possibility that the specificity of the beginning is a condition or the specificity of return is an obstacle, or it prevents the non-ability of the servant to recreate is nothing because the opponent acknowledges the two premises. And among them: that if he were capable of bringing his action into existence, he would be capable of bringing its like into existence because the rule of similars is one, yet we are certain that it is impossible for us now to do the same as we did previously without difference, even if we exert effort in deliberation and precaution. And among them: that if he were capable of bringing his action into existence, he would be capable of bringing every possible thing into existence from bodies and accidents because the corrector of being an object of power is possibility or origination, and the object of power is bestowing existence, and there is no difference in any of them according to the opponent's admission, and the refutation does not hold.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage presents several proofs from early scholars (al-mutaqaddimin) to support the Ash'ari position that the servant's action is created by God's power, not by the servant's own power. The first proof is stated briefly: if the servant were capable of his action, he would be capable of recreating it, and also of creating a body (since there is no distinct cause for origination and possibility), and his action would be comparable to God's creation—but he cannot do such things. The text then elaborates: the early scholars give multiple 'wujuh' (aspects). The first aspect: if the servant were truly the originator (ijadan wa ikhtira'an) of his act, he would be able to recreate it; but this is impossible by consensus. The implication (wajh al-luzum) is that the possibility of power over a thing continues as long as the thing's essence is possible, and essence does not change with time. Thus, just as God's power to begin creation implies His power to recreate, so it would be for the servant. An objection is raised: the recreation of a non-existent thing may be prevented by specific conditions. The response dismisses this because the opponent already admits the two premises (that power over the first implies power over the second). The second proof: if one can create an action, one can create an exactly similar action (because the rule of similars is one), but we know empirically that we cannot repeat an action without any difference, no matter how hard we try. The third proof: if one could create his own action, he could create all possible things (bodies and accidents), because the ground for being an object of power is either possibility or origination, and there is no difference among possible things in this regard. Therefore, the claim that the servant has power over his action leads to absurd consequences. The text cuts off at 'wa la yarid al-naqd' (and the refutation does not hold) which likely continues.

Key Terms
فعل العبد
إيجاد مثله
حكم الأمثال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Humans can repeat actions exactly if they try hard enough.
Correction: No matter how much effort we exert, we cannot produce an action that is completely identical in all aspects to a previous one. This shows we are not the true creators.

Checkpoint
What logical principle does the second proof use to argue that if the servant could create his action, he could create its like?
A) The principle of sufficient reason
B) The rule of similars (hukm al-amthal wahid)
C) Occam's razor
D) The law of non-contradiction

Key Takeaway
The second proof shows that the inability to create an exactly similar action reveals that we are not the originators of our actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you plan an action to perfection but it still turns out differently, does it unsettle you to realize you are not the ultimate creator? How does that change the way you rely on Allah?
?
If you truly believe you create your own actions, why can you not repeat a good deed with identical quality? Does this expose a hidden pride in your own ability?
?
This proof shows that even your best effort cannot produce an identical action. Does that humble you enough to beg Allah to make your actions acceptable, because they are not truly yours?

The Reductio Against Human Creation of Actions
Argument by reductio against human creative power

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a reductio ad absurdum argument is used to disprove the claim that humans create their own actions.

Core Idea
If humans created their good deeds, they would surpass God in goodness—an impossibility.

This passage presents a logical proof by contradiction against the view that human actions are self-created. It contrasts human acts of obedience with divine creations like devils, arguing that if humans were the source of their own good works, they would surpass God in excellence. Read carefully to see how this argument unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal translation. Notice the logical structure: a premise, an implication, and a conclusion.
The argument is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the premise
Find the premise that human good deeds (faith, obedience) are better than divine creations (bodies, devils). The author states 'there is no doubt that the first is better than the second.'
This premise is comparative.
3
Trace the logical implication
If humans were the creators of their own good deeds, then their creation would be better than God's creation. This would make humans superior to God in creation, reform, and guidance.
This is the 'if' part of the reductio.
4
Recognize the absurdity and conclusion
The conclusion that humans could be better than God is absurd. Therefore, the assumption that humans create their own actions must be false. The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.
This is the core of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالقدرة الاكتسابية (۱) لأنها تتعلق بالذوات وأحوالها وهي مختلفة، ومنها أن من فعل العبد الإيمان والطاعات وكثير من الحسنات ومن خلق الله تعالى الأجسام والأعراض والشياطين وكثير من المؤذيات، ولا شك أن الأول أحسن من الثاني وأشرف ، فلو كان العبد خالقاً لفعله لكان أحسن وأشرف من الله تعالى خلقاً وإصلاحاً وإرشاداً.
By the acquisitive power (1) because it pertains to essences and their states, and they are diverse. Among them: that of the slave’s act are faith, acts of obedience, and many good deeds; and of God’s creation are bodies, accidents, devils, and many harmful things. There is no doubt that the first is better and more noble than the second. So if the slave were the creator of his own act, he would be better and more noble than God in creation, reform, and guidance.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage presents an argument against the view that humans create their own actions. It begins by stating that the 'acquisitive power' (al-qudra al-iktisābiyya) pertains to essences and their states, which are diverse. Then it gives an example: human actions include faith, obedience, and good deeds, while God’s creations include bodies, accidents, devils, and harmful things. Obviously, the former (human good actions) are better and more noble than the latter (devils, etc.). Therefore, if the slave were the creator of his own good actions, he would be better than God in creation, reform, and guidance—which is absurd. This is a reductio ad absurdum to show that humans cannot be the creators of their own actions.

Key Terms
قدرة اكتسابية
خالق
فعل العبد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument claims that humans are actually better than God.
Correction: This is a logical proof by contradiction; the absurd conclusion is intended to disprove the premise that humans create their actions.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the argument in this passage?
A) Humans are better than God in creation.
B) If humans were the creators of their good actions, they would be superior to God—an impossibility.
C) The power of acquisition is the same as divine power.
D) God creates only harmful things.

Key Takeaway
The argument uses the superiority of human good deeds over divine creations to prove, by reductio ad absurdum, that humans cannot be the creators of their own actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage argues that if you created your own good deeds, you would surpass God in goodness. That is impossible. So what reality does this force you to accept about your own power? How would living in that reality transform your humility and trust in God?

Objection: Is the Power to Faith Better Than Faith?
Objection and answer regarding the superiority of power to faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a key objection against Ash'ari theology regarding the power to faith and its refutation through a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Core Idea
If power to faith is better, then power to evil is worse than disbelief.

A common objection to predestinarian theology is that God's creating the power to faith is a greater good than faith itself, since faith depends on it. In this passage, Sanusi presents the objection and then refutes it with a logical parallel that shows the absurd consequence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the claim that the power to faith is better, clearer, and more beneficial than faith because faith depends on it.
The objector assumes that the means is better than the end.
2
Analyze the logic of the objection
Why does the objector think power is better? Because faith relies on God's creation of that power.
3
Read the answer and identify the reductio
The answer draws an analogy: if the premise holds, then power to evil would be worse than disbelief. That is absurd.
This is a logical refutation, not a denial of divine power.
4
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider why this reductio works: it shows the premise leads to a contradiction, so the premise must be false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : القدرة على الإيمان أحسن وأوضح وأصلح من الإيمان لتوقفه عليها بخلق الله تعالى . قلنا : فيلزم أن تكون القدرة على الشر والتمكن منه شراً من الكفر وأقبح منه.
If it is said: The power to faith is better, clearer, and more beneficial than faith because faith depends on it by God’s creation. We say: Then it would follow that the power to evil and the ability to do it is worse than disbelief and more ugly than it.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This is an objection and answer. The objector argues: The power (qudra) to have faith is better than faith itself because faith depends on that power, which is created by God. So if God creates the power, He is the source of goodness. The answer refutes by analogy: If the power to faith is better than faith, then by the same logic, the power to evil (sharr) and the ability to commit it would be worse than disbelief (kufr) itself. But that is absurd, because disbelief is the worst thing. Therefore, the premise leads to a contradiction, so the objection is invalid.

Key Terms
قدرة
إيمان
كفر
شر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The power to faith is intrinsically better than faith itself.
Correction: This leads to the absurdity that the power to evil would be worse than disbelief. The correct view is that faith is better than the power to it.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the answer to the objection?
A) The power to faith is indeed better than faith.
B) If power to faith were better, then power to evil would be worse than disbelief, which is unacceptable.
C) God does not create the power to evil.
D) Faith does not depend on God's power.

Key Takeaway
The power to faith cannot be better than faith, otherwise the power to evil would be worse than disbelief, an absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever take pride in your ability to do good, thinking that the potential itself makes you better than others? How does this lesson challenge the value you place on potential versus action?
?
Consider a specific sin you have the power to commit but haven't. Do you secretly feel that your restraint makes you more virtuous? What does the absurdity in this passage reveal about that feeling?
?
If the power to faith is not better than faith itself, then what does that demand from you today? Are you content with mere potential, or are you actively exercising your faith right now?

Argument from Consensus on Supplication for Faith
Sanusi presents the consensus on supplication as proof that faith is created by God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the consensus on supplication (tadarruʿ) for faith proves that faith is entirely created by God.

Core Idea
Supplication for faith only makes sense if faith comes from God, not from ourselves.

One of the powerful arguments used by Ashʿarī theologians is the consensus of the Muslim community on the validity of supplicating to God for faith and obedience. This lesson unpacks that argument, showing how it logically forces us to affirm that all acts, including faith, are created by God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Familiarize yourself with the text and translation; note key terms like umma, tadarruʿ, iqdār, tamkīn, taqdīr, tathbīt.
2
Identify the core claim
The community agrees that supplication for faith is valid. This validity depends on faith being created by God.
3
Analyze the refutation
The passage rejects alternative interpretations (empowerment, predestination) and shows they lead to contradiction.
4
Connect to Ashʿarī doctrine
This argument supports the view that all actions are created by God, with human acquisition as secondary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن الأمة مجموعة على صحة تضرع العبد إلى الله تعالى في أن يرزقه الإيمان والطاعة، ويجنبه الكفر والمعصية، ولولا أن الكل بخلق الله تعالى لما صح ذلك. إذ لا وجه لحمله على سؤال الإقدار والتمكين لأنه حاصل أو التقدير والتثبيت لأنه عائد إلى الحصول في الزمان الثاني، وذلك عندهم بقدرة العبد، ومنها أن(۲) الأمة مجمعون على صحة ذلك بل وجوب حمد الله وشكره على نعمة الإيمان نفسه، ذلك إلا إذا كان بخلقه وإعطائه وإن كان لكسب العبد مدخل فيه، فأما يتصور ، ولا الشكر على مقدماته من الإقدار والتمكين والتوفيق والتعريف ونحو ذلك فشيء آخر
Among them: that the community (umma) is agreed upon the validity of the slave’s supplication to God to grant him faith and obedience, and to protect him from disbelief and disobedience; and were it not that all is by God’s creation, that would not be valid. For there is no way to interpret it as a request for empowerment and enablement because that is already obtained, or for predestination and confirmation because that reverts to obtaining in the second moment, and that according to them is by the slave’s power. And among them: that the community is agreed upon the validity of that, rather the obligation of praising and thanking God for the blessing of faith itself, that is unless it is by His creation and giving, even if the slave’s acquisition has a role in it. As for thanking for its preliminaries of empowerment, enablement, divine success, and guidance and the like, that is something else.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments from consensus (ijmāʿ). First, the entire Muslim community agrees that it is valid for a slave to supplicate (tadarruʿ) to God to grant him faith and obedience and to protect him from disbelief and sin. If faith and sin were not entirely created by God, such supplication would be meaningless, because the slave would be asking God to create something that the slave himself creates. The objector might say the supplication is for empowerment (iqdār) or enablement (tamkīn), but that is already present, or for predestination (taqdīr) or confirmation (tathbīt), but that refers to future acquisition by the slave’s power—thus it still implies the act is from the slave, not from God. So the consensus on supplication proves that all is from God. Second, the community agrees on the obligation to praise and thank God for the blessing of faith itself. If faith were not God’s creation and gift, thanking Him for it would not be necessary. The passage notes that even if the slave’s acquisition (kasb) plays a role, the primary creation is from God. Thanking for the preliminaries is another matter.

Key Terms
أمة
تضرع
إقدار
تمكين
تقدير
تثبيت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Supplication for faith is merely asking for God's help while the act is from human free will.
Correction: The passage argues that if the act were from human free will, supplication would be unnecessary; the community's consensus on its validity shows the act is entirely from God.

Checkpoint
Why does the consensus on supplication for faith prove that faith is created by God?
A) Because the slave would be asking for something he can create himself, which is absurd.
B) Because supplication is only valid for material things.
C) Because the community does not actually agree on supplication.
D) Because God only grants faith to prophets.

Key Takeaway
The consensus on supplication for faith demonstrates that faith must be entirely created by God, otherwise the supplication would be incoherent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sincerely supplicate for faith or guidance, do you truly believe that God alone creates that faith in your heart? Or do you think it depends on your own effort? How does this affect the humility of your du'a? Will you now begin each du'a with the awareness that you are asking God to create something in you, not just to help what you already have?
?
Consider a specific sin you struggled with and then stopped. Did you credit yourself for overcoming it, or did you thank God for protecting you? According to this passage, what must you acknowledge about the source of your obedience? Are you willing to attribute both your faith and your avoidance of sin entirely to God's creation, even while feeling your own responsibility?
?
If the entire umma agrees on the validity of supplication for faith, why do you sometimes hesitate to make du'a for your own faith when it weakens? What does this hesitation reveal about your underlying belief regarding who controls your heart? Will you commit to making a daily du'a for God to grant you faith and keep you from disbelief, fully trusting that He alone can answer it?

Argument from Consensus on Thanking God for Faith
Sanusi argues from the consensus on thanking God for faith that faith is God's creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the consensus on thanking God for faith itself proves that faith is a direct gift from God and not merely a human achievement.

Core Idea
Thanking God for faith only makes sense if faith itself is His creation and bestowal.

The second argument from consensus focuses on the universal obligation to praise and thank God for the blessing of faith (īmān). This lesson explores why such gratitude is only rational if faith comes from God, even while acknowledging a role for human acquisition (kasb).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Note the shift from supplication to gratitude. The passage insists that gratitude for faith itself is obligatory.
2
Understand the key condition
The phrase 'that is only if it is by His creation and giving' is the core of the argument. Gratitude for faith is valid only if faith is God's gift.
3
Distinguish between faith and its preliminaries
The author clarifies that thanking for empowerment, enablement, etc., is a different matter. This sharpens the argument.
4
Connect to the doctrine of kasb
Even though human acquisition (kasb) plays a role, the primary giver is God. This balances divine omnipotence and human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن الأمة مجموعة على صحة تضرع العبد إلى الله تعالى في أن يرزقه الإيمان والطاعة، ويجنبه الكفر والمعصية، ولولا أن الكل بخلق الله تعالى لما صح ذلك. إذ لا وجه لحمله على سؤال الإقدار والتمكين لأنه حاصل أو التقدير والتثبيت لأنه عائد إلى الحصول في الزمان الثاني، وذلك عندهم بقدرة العبد، ومنها أن(۲) الأمة مجمعون على صحة ذلك بل وجوب حمد الله وشكره على نعمة الإيمان نفسه، ذلك إلا إذا كان بخلقه وإعطائه وإن كان لكسب العبد مدخل فيه، فأما يتصور ، ولا الشكر على مقدماته من الإقدار والتمكين والتوفيق والتعريف ونحو ذلك فشيء آخر
Among them: that the community (umma) is agreed upon the validity of the slave’s supplication to God to grant him faith and obedience, and to protect him from disbelief and disobedience; and were it not that all is by God’s creation, that would not be valid. For there is no way to interpret it as a request for empowerment and enablement because that is already obtained, or for predestination and confirmation because that reverts to obtaining in the second moment, and that according to them is by the slave’s power. And among them: that the community is agreed upon the validity of that, rather the obligation of praising and thanking God for the blessing of faith itself, that is unless it is by His creation and giving, even if the slave’s acquisition has a role in it. As for thanking for its preliminaries of empowerment, enablement, divine success, and guidance and the like, that is something else.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments from consensus (ijmāʿ). First, the entire Muslim community agrees that it is valid for a slave to supplicate (tadarruʿ) to God to grant him faith and obedience and to protect him from disbelief and sin. If faith and sin were not entirely created by God, such supplication would be meaningless, because the slave would be asking God to create something that the slave himself creates. The objector might say the supplication is for empowerment (iqdār) or enablement (tamkīn), but that is already present, or for predestination (taqdīr) or confirmation (tathbīt), but that refers to future acquisition by the slave’s power—thus it still implies the act is from the slave, not from God. So the consensus on supplication proves that all is from God. Second, the community agrees on the obligation to praise and thank God for the blessing of faith itself. If faith were not God’s creation and gift, thanking Him for it would not be necessary. The passage notes that even if the slave’s acquisition (kasb) plays a role, the primary creation is from God. Thanking for the preliminaries is another matter.

Key Terms
أمة
حمد
شكر
نعمة الإيمان
كسب
إقدار
تمكين
توفيق
تعريف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thanking God for faith is about thanking for the ability to act, not for the act itself.
Correction: The passage specifies that the gratitude is for the 'blessing of faith itself' (niʿmat al-īmān nafsihi), which is only valid if faith is created by God.

Checkpoint
Why does the obligation to thank God for faith itself prove that faith is created by God?
A) Because God commands gratitude for everything.
B) Because gratitude is only due for gifts from others, not for one's own actions.
C) Because the community also agrees on supplication.
D) Because the slave's acquisition has no role.

Key Takeaway
The consensus on thanking God for faith itself establishes that faith is a direct divine gift, created and given by God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your faith is entirely a gift from God, or do you secretly feel that you deserve some credit for your own belief? How would your daily prayers change if you genuinely internalized that even your ability to pray is a direct creation of God?
?
When you thank God for your faith, do you actually mean the faith itself, or are you merely thanking Him for making belief easy for you? What would it mean to thank Him for the very act of believing, moment by moment, as an ongoing gift?
?
If every aspect of your iman is created and given by God, then what room remains for pride, despair, or self-reliance? Will you commit today to asking God for faith itself—not just for the circumstances that help you believe—as the greatest of His blessings?

If Creating Faith Merits Praise, Then Creating Disbelief Merits Blame?
Objection and answer about divine creation and moral judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Muslim theologians defend God's absolute ownership against the objection that creating both good and evil would entail blame for evil.

Core Idea
God's ownership exempts His actions from human standards of praise and blame.

This lesson presents a classic objection against the Ash'ari view that God creates both good and evil actions. The objector argues that if God deserves praise for creating faith, then He must deserve blame for creating disbelief. The response hinges on God's absolute ownership and authority, which places Him beyond the moral categories applicable to human beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: objection (فإن قيل) and answer (قلنا).
The answer begins with ممنوع (it is denied).
2
Understand the objector's logic
The objector uses a parity argument: if creating good (faith) merits praise, then creating evil (disbelief) must merit blame by the same reasoning.
3
See how the answer denies the premise
The answer does not deny that God creates disbelief; it denies that the moral analogy holds. Instead, it distinguishes God's actions from human actions based on God's unique status.
4
Grasp the grounding in divine ownership
The answer grounds the distinction in God's absolute ownership (المالك). Because He is the Owner, His actions are not subject to human moral judgment. The phrase 'فله الأمر كله' (to Him belongs the entire command) is key.
5
Reflect on theological implications
This response clarifies that God's creation of evil is not morally blameworthy because God is not subject to the same moral law as His creatures. This upholds God's transcendence and sovereignty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لو استحق بخلق الإيمان المدح لاستحق بخلق الكفر الذم . قلنا : ممنوع فإن من شأنه استحقاق المدح والشكر بخلق الحسنات، وإيصال النعم لا الذم بخلق القبائح وإرسال النقم، لأنه المالك فله الأمر كله، لا يقبح منه خلق القبيح .
If it is said: If He deserved praise for creating faith, then He would deserve blame for creating disbelief. We say: It is denied. For it is His characteristic to deserve praise and thanks for creating good deeds and conveying blessings, not blame for creating ugly deeds and sending calamities, because He is the Owner; to Him belongs the entire command. It is not ugly for Him to create the ugly.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This is another objection and answer. The objector argues by parity of reasoning: if God deserves praise (maddḥ) for creating faith, then He must deserve blame (dhamm) for creating disbelief. The answer denies the premise. It explains that God’s nature is such that He deserves praise and thanks for creating good deeds (ḥasanāt) and bestowing blessings (niʿam), but He does not deserve blame for creating ugly deeds (qabāʾiḥ) or sending calamities (niqam), because He is the absolute Owner (mālik). He has full authority over His creation. It is not considered ugly for the Owner to create what is ugly; moral categories do not apply to Him in the same way they apply to slaves.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this passage implies God does not create evil or that He is blameworthy for creating disbelief.
Correction: The passage does not deny that God creates disbelief; rather it denies that human moral categories apply to God. God is the Owner and His actions are beyond praise and blame.

Checkpoint
Why does the answer deny that God deserves blame for creating disbelief?
A) Because God does not actually create disbelief.
B) Because God is the owner and has absolute authority, so His actions are not subject to human standards of blame.
C) Because disbelief is not ugly to God.
D) Because the objector misunderstood the nature of faith.

Key Takeaway
God's absolute ownership means that His creation of both good and evil is not subject to human standards of blame or praise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face hardships or see evil in the world, do you find yourself questioning God's justice? How does knowing that God is the absolute Owner and that His actions are beyond human moral categories reshape your trust in Him, even when you don't understand?
?

?
You have heard that God is the Owner; does your life reflect that ownership? Are there areas of your life where you act as if you own your own successes or failures, instead of submitting to His absolute command? What would it look like to truly live under His ownership today?

The Ambiguity of 'Faith is Created'
Objection about the ruling on saying 'faith is created', and the nuanced answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the statement 'faith is created' can be considered disbelief in some contexts, and how al-Nasafi's distinction between divine and human aspects resolves the ambiguity.

Core Idea
Faith is not entirely from God nor entirely from the slave; each contributes different aspects.

This lesson addresses a famous objection about a fatwa that declares saying 'faith is created' to be disbelief. The answer from Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi reveals a crucial distinction: certain divine aspects of faith are uncreated, while human aspects are created. This nuanced understanding avoids both Jabri and Qadari extremes.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from objection to answer.
The passage is a classic objection-and-answer format in kalam.
2
Identify the two extremes
The answer clarifies that faith is not entirely from God (Jabr) nor entirely from the slave (Qadar). Understand each extreme briefly.
Jabr: God compels actions; Qadar: slave independently creates actions.
3
Divide faith's components
Al-Nasafi lists four aspects from God (uncreated) and four from the slave (created). Note each list and its reference to divine origination (takwin) or human creation.
Divine: ta'rif, tawfiq, hidaya, i'ta'. Human: ma'rifa, qasd, i'tidad, qubul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فعندكم الإيمان مخلوق الله تعالى وعندهم مخلوق العبد، وقد ذكر في بعض الفتاوى أن من قال : الإيمان مخلوق كفر، فما وجهه . . . ؟ قلنا وجهه ما أشار إليه أبو المعين النسفي (۱) رحمه الله من أن الإيمان ليس كله من الله إلى العبد على ما هو الجبر، ولا من العبد على ما هو ،القدر، بل من الله التعريف والتوفيق والهداية والإعطاء ومرجعها إلى التكوين وهو غير مخلوق ومن العبد المعرفة والقصد والاعتداد والقبول وهي مخلوقة.
If it is said: According to you, faith is created by God, and according to them, it is created by the slave. And it is mentioned in some fatwas that whoever says 'faith is created' disbelieves. So what is its explanation? We say: Its explanation is what Abu al-Mu’in al-Nasafi (1) may God have mercy on him indicated: that faith is not entirely from God to the slave as in jabr (compulsion), nor entirely from the slave as in qadar (free will), but from God are definition (taʿrīf), divine success (tawfīq), guidance (hidāya), and giving (iʿṭā’), whose reference is to origination (takwīn), which is uncreated; and from the slave are knowledge (maʿrifa), intention (qaṣd), esteem (iʿtidād), and acceptance (qubūl), which are created.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This is an objection and answer regarding the statement 'faith is created'. The objector notes that some fatwas declare that saying 'faith is created' is disbelief (kufr). Since both the Maturidites (the author's school) and the Mu'tazilites say faith is created (by God or by the slave respectively), why would that be disbelief? The answer clarifies using the view of Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi. He explains that faith is not entirely from God (which would be jabr, compulsion) nor entirely from the slave (which would be qadar, free will in a sense that denies divine creation). Instead, certain aspects come from God: taʿrīf (definition/identification), tawfīq (divine success), hidāya (guidance), and iʿṭā’ (giving), which all refer to takwīn (origination), and these are uncreated (ghayr makhliq). Other aspects come from the slave: maʿrifa (knowledge), qaṣd (intention), iʿtidād (esteem/accepting as significant), and qubūl (acceptance), and these are created. Thus, the claim that 'faith is created' is ambiguous: if one intends the entire faith, it might imply creaturely control, while if one distinguishes, it avoids heresy. The fatwa probably targets those who say the whole faith is created in a way that denies divine action.

مخلوق

قدر
تعريف
توفيق
هداية
إعطاء
تكوين
معرفة
قصد
اعتداد
قبول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the fatwa forbids any statement that faith is created, even if one distinguishes divine and human aspects.
Correction: The fatwa targets an unqualified statement implying faith is entirely created by the slave, denying divine action. Al-Nasafi's distinction shows that part of faith is uncreated, so the statement is only kufr if it denies God's role.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi's explanation, which aspects of faith are created?
A) All of faith is created.
B) None of faith is created.
C) The divine aspects are created, but the human aspects are uncreated.
D) The human aspects (knowledge, intention, etc.) are created, while the divine aspects (guidance, success) are uncreated.

Key Takeaway
The statement 'faith is created' is ambiguous; understanding al-Nasafi's distinction prevents misinterpretation and avoids extremes of Jabr and Qadar.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your faith, do you see it as a gift from God (tawfīq) or as something you earned by your own knowledge (maʿrifa)? Which aspect do you tend to neglect, and how does that affect your gratitude or pride?
?
The passage warns against two extremes: blaming God for lack of faith (Jabr) or taking full credit (Qadar). Honestly, which extreme do you lean toward in your heart? What would it look like for you to actively embrace both divine giving and your own responsibility today?
?
If someone asked, 'Who is ultimately responsible for your faith?' what would you answer based on this lesson? Is your daily practice of faith reflecting a balance—relying on God's guidance while exerting your own intention and acceptance? If not, what one concrete change will you make this week to live out this truth?

The Two Interpretations of 'What You Do'
Explains the two interpretations of 'ma' in the verse and their support for the Ash'ari doctrine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic verse 'And Allah created you and what you do' supports the Ash'ari doctrine that Allah creates all human acts, through the two grammatical interpretations of the word 'ma'.

Core Idea
The word 'ma' in the verse can mean 'your action' or 'what you do', both pointing to Allah as the creator of deeds.

This lesson examines the second proof from the Qur'an for the doctrine that Allah creates all human acts. We will explore the verse 'And Allah created you and what you do' and analyze the two grammatical interpretations of the word 'ma' — as a masdariyya (infinitive) or a mawṣūla (relative pronoun) — and how both support the Ash'ari position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author presents two possible meanings for 'ma'.
2
Understand the two interpretations
The author explains that if 'ma' is masdariyya, the meaning is directly 'He created your action'. If it is mawṣūla, it includes all actions acquired by the servant. Identify these two cases and see how each leads to the same conclusion.
The dispute is about the acquired acts, not the act of bringing into existence (īqāʿ).
3
See the verse in context
The author cites other verses like 'they do righteous deeds' to show that the dispute is about these acquired acts. Notice how he emphasises that the subtle point is often missed.
This connects the interpretation to the broader theological debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليل الثاني
قال (ومنها قوله تعالى والله خلقكم وما تعملون) .
إما على المصدرية المستغنية عن الإضمار فظاهر، وإما على الموصولية فلشمولها الأفعال التي يكتسبها العبد من الحركات والسكنات والأوضاع والهيئات كما في قوله تعالى وعملوا الصالحات ويعملون السيئات إذ فيها النزاع لا في الإيقاع
أما إذا كانت (ما) مصدرية على ما اختاره سيبويه لاستغنائه عن الحذف والإضمار فالأمر ظاهر لأن المعنى وخلق عملكم، وأما إذا كانت موصولة على حذف الضمير، أي: وخلق ما تعملونه بقرينة قوله تعالى أتعبدون ما تنحتون توبيخاً لهم على عبادة ما عملوه من الأصنام فلأن كلمة (ما) عامة تتناول ما يعملونها من الأوضاع، والحركات والمعاصي والطاعات وغير ذلك. فإن المراد بأفعال العباد المختلف في كونها بخلق العبد أو بخلق الرب هو ما يقع بكسب العبد، ويستند إليه مثل الصوم والصلاة والأكل والشرب والقيام والقعود ونحو ذلك مما يسمي الحاصل بالمصدر لا نفس الإيقاع الذي هو من الاعتبارات العقلية الا يرى إلى مثل يقيمون الصلاة، ويفعلون الزكاة ويعملون الصالحات والسيئات وهذه النكته مما غفل عنها الجمهور، فبالغوا في نفي كون (ما) موصولة حتى صرح الإمام بأن مثل ما تنحتون، وما يأفكون في قوله تعالى فإذا هي تلقف ما يأفكون مجاز دفعاً للاشتراك .
واما اعتراضهم بأن الآية حجة عليكم لا لكم حيث أسند العبادة والنحت والعمل إلى المخاطبين فجهل بالمتنازع .
The Second Proof: He said: Among them is His saying, exalted be He, 'And Allah created you and what you do' (Surah al-Saffat 96). Either it is taken as a masdariyya (infinitive) that is independent of the implied object, then it is apparent; or it is taken as a mawṣūla (relative pronoun), then it includes the actions acquired by the servant — movements, stillnesses, positions, and states — just as in His saying 'and they do righteous deeds' and 'they do evil deeds', for the dispute is about these, not about the actual bringing into existence (īqāʿ). As for when 'mā' is masdariyya according to Sibawayh's choice because it is independent of deletion and implication, the matter is apparent, for the meaning is 'and He created your action'. As for when it is mawṣūla with the deletion of the implied pronoun, i.e. 'and He created what you do', by the evidence of His saying 'Do you worship what you carve?' (Surah al-Saffat 95), as a rebuke to them for worshipping what they made of idols, then the word 'mā' is general, encompassing what they do of positions, movements, sins, acts of obedience, and other things. For the intended meaning of the acts of servants concerning which there is disagreement whether they are created by the servant or by the Lord is what occurs through the servant's acquisition (kasb) and is attributed to him, such as fasting, prayer, eating, drinking, standing, sitting, and the like — what is called the product (ḥāṣil) by the verbal noun — not the actual bringing into existence (īqāʿ) which is among rational considerations. Do you not see such as 'they establish prayer', 'they give zakah', and 'they do righteous deeds and evil deeds'? This subtle point is something the majority have overlooked, so they exaggerated in denying that 'mā' is mawṣūla, to the point that the Imām declared that such as 'what you carve' and 'what they conjure' in His saying 'Behold it swallows up what they conjure' (Surah al-A'raf 117) is figurative to avoid homonymy. As for their objection that the verse is an argument against you, not for you, because it ascribes worship, carving, and work to those addressed — that is ignorance of the point at issue.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the doctrine that Allah creates all human acts. It begins by citing the Quranic verse 'And Allah created you and what you do' (Surah al-Saffat 96). The author then analyzes the word 'mā' in the verse, explaining that it can be interpreted either as a masdariyya (infinitive particle) or as a mawṣūla (relative pronoun). If it is masdariyya, the meaning is 'and He created your action', which directly affirms that Allah creates the action. If it is mawṣūla, it includes all actions acquired by the servant — movements, stillness, postures, etc. — as evidenced by other verses like 'and they do righteous deeds' and 'they do evil deeds'. The author emphasizes that the dispute is about these acquired acts, not about the actual bringing into existence (īqāʿ) which is a rational concept. He then notes a subtlety that many have missed: they denied that 'mā' could be mawṣūla, forcing them to interpret some Quranic usages as figurative. Finally, he addresses an objection: that the verse attributes the actions to the people addressed, so it would be evidence against the Ashʿari position. The author dismisses this as ignorance of the matter under dispute, because the ascription of the act to the human through acquisition (kasb) does not negate that its creation is from Allah.

Key Terms
ما المصدرية
ما الموصولة
الكسب
الإيقاع
الخلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main point of contention regarding human actions according to the author?
A) Whether actions exist at all
B) Whether the actual bringing into existence (īqāʿ) is created by Allah or the servant
C) Whether the acquired acts (al-afʿāl al-muktasaba) are created by Allah or the servant
D) Whether the word 'mā' in the verse is a relative pronoun or an infinitive particle

Key Takeaway
Both interpretations of 'mā' in the verse affirm that Allah creates human actions, though the actions are acquired by humans.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you truly feel the weight of it, knowing that Allah created even that action? How does that affect your sense of accountability before Him?
?
The author points out a subtlety that many scholars missed. In your own life, what 'subtle points' have you overlooked that could have drawn you closer to Allah or clarified your faith?
?
If every action you do, from standing to sitting, is created by Allah, then what is your real contribution? Does this humble you or make you feel passive? What does your intention (niyya) actually add?

Refuting the Objection to the Verse
Refutes the objection that the verse is evidence against Ash'ari by clarifying the role of acquisition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that the verse attributes actions to humans is refuted by the distinction between creation and acquisition (kasb).

Core Idea
The verse attributes actions to humans through acquisition, not creation, so the objection misses the point.

In this lesson, we continue with the second proof by addressing a common objection: that the verse 'And Allah created you and what you do' actually proves humans create their own actions because it says 'what you do'. We will see how the author refutes this by clarifying that the ascription is through acquisition (kasb), not creation, and that the real dispute is about the origin of the acquired acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and refutation
Read the passage that presents the objection and the author's response. The objection claims that the verse is evidence against the Ash'ari position because it attributes actions to humans.
Note how the author dismisses the objection as ignorance of the matter under dispute.
2
Understand the concept of acquisition (kasb)
The author explains that the actions attributed to humans in the verse are those acquired by them, not those brought into existence by them. This is a key distinction in Ash'ari theology.
The actual bringing into existence (īqāʿ) is from Allah alone.
3
Apply the distinction to the objection
Realise that the objection confuses the two levels: the verse speaks of what humans do in terms of acquisition, not creation. Thus, it does not contradict the doctrine that Allah creates all actions.
This distinction resolves the apparent contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليل الثاني
قال (ومنها قوله تعالى والله خلقكم وما تعملون) .
إما على المصدرية المستغنية عن الإضمار فظاهر، وإما على الموصولية فلشمولها الأفعال التي يكتسبها العبد من الحركات والسكنات والأوضاع والهيئات كما في قوله تعالى وعملوا الصالحات ويعملون السيئات إذ فيها النزاع لا في الإيقاع
أما إذا كانت (ما) مصدرية على ما اختاره سيبويه لاستغنائه عن الحذف والإضمار فالأمر ظاهر لأن المعنى وخلق عملكم، وأما إذا كانت موصولة على حذف الضمير، أي: وخلق ما تعملونه بقرينة قوله تعالى أتعبدون ما تنحتون توبيخاً لهم على عبادة ما عملوه من الأصنام فلأن كلمة (ما) عامة تتناول ما يعملونها من الأوضاع، والحركات والمعاصي والطاعات وغير ذلك. فإن المراد بأفعال العباد المختلف في كونها بخلق العبد أو بخلق الرب هو ما يقع بكسب العبد، ويستند إليه مثل الصوم والصلاة والأكل والشرب والقيام والقعود ونحو ذلك مما يسمي الحاصل بالمصدر لا نفس الإيقاع الذي هو من الاعتبارات العقلية الا يرى إلى مثل يقيمون الصلاة، ويفعلون الزكاة ويعملون الصالحات والسيئات وهذه النكته مما غفل عنها الجمهور، فبالغوا في نفي كون (ما) موصولة حتى صرح الإمام بأن مثل ما تنحتون، وما يأفكون في قوله تعالى فإذا هي تلقف ما يأفكون مجاز دفعاً للاشتراك .
واما اعتراضهم بأن الآية حجة عليكم لا لكم حيث أسند العبادة والنحت والعمل إلى المخاطبين فجهل بالمتنازع .
The Second Proof: He said: Among them is His saying, exalted be He, 'And Allah created you and what you do' (Surah al-Saffat 96). Either it is taken as a masdariyya (infinitive) that is independent of the implied object, then it is apparent; or it is taken as a mawṣūla (relative pronoun), then it includes the actions acquired by the servant — movements, stillnesses, positions, and states — just as in His saying 'and they do righteous deeds' and 'they do evil deeds', for the dispute is about these, not about the actual bringing into existence (īqāʿ). As for when 'mā' is masdariyya according to Sibawayh's choice because it is independent of deletion and implication, the matter is apparent, for the meaning is 'and He created your action'. As for when it is mawṣūla with the deletion of the implied pronoun, i.e. 'and He created what you do', by the evidence of His saying 'Do you worship what you carve?' (Surah al-Saffat 95), as a rebuke to them for worshipping what they made of idols, then the word 'mā' is general, encompassing what they do of positions, movements, sins, acts of obedience, and other things. For the intended meaning of the acts of servants concerning which there is disagreement whether they are created by the servant or by the Lord is what occurs through the servant's acquisition (kasb) and is attributed to him, such as fasting, prayer, eating, drinking, standing, sitting, and the like — what is called the product (ḥāṣil) by the verbal noun — not the actual bringing into existence (īqāʿ) which is among rational considerations. Do you not see such as 'they establish prayer', 'they give zakah', and 'they do righteous deeds and evil deeds'? This subtle point is something the majority have overlooked, so they exaggerated in denying that 'mā' is mawṣūla, to the point that the Imām declared that such as 'what you carve' and 'what they conjure' in His saying 'Behold it swallows up what they conjure' (Surah al-A'raf 117) is figurative to avoid homonymy. As for their objection that the verse is an argument against you, not for you, because it ascribes worship, carving, and work to those addressed — that is ignorance of the point at issue.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the doctrine that Allah creates all human acts. It begins by citing the Quranic verse 'And Allah created you and what you do' (Surah al-Saffat 96). The author then analyzes the word 'mā' in the verse, explaining that it can be interpreted either as a masdariyya (infinitive particle) or as a mawṣūla (relative pronoun). If it is masdariyya, the meaning is 'and He created your action', which directly affirms that Allah creates the action. If it is mawṣūla, it includes all actions acquired by the servant — movements, stillness, postures, etc. — as evidenced by other verses like 'and they do righteous deeds' and 'they do evil deeds'. The author emphasizes that the dispute is about these acquired acts, not about the actual bringing into existence (īqāʿ) which is a rational concept. He then notes a subtlety that many have missed: they denied that 'mā' could be mawṣūla, forcing them to interpret some Quranic usages as figurative. Finally, he addresses an objection: that the verse attributes the actions to the people addressed, so it would be evidence against the Ashʿari position. The author dismisses this as ignorance of the matter under dispute, because the ascription of the act to the human through acquisition (kasb) does not negate that its creation is from Allah.

Key Terms
الكسب
الإيقاع
الخلق
اعتراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse attributes actions to humans, so it proves humans create their own actions.
Correction: The verse attributes actions through acquisition (kasb), not creation. The dispute is about the creation of the acquired acts, not the ascription of the act to the human.

Checkpoint
Why does the author dismiss the objection that the verse is evidence against the Ash'ari position?
A) Because the verse has a different context
B) Because the objection confuses creation with acquisition
C) Because the opponents cannot read Arabic
D) Because the objection is not mentioned in the Qur'an

Key Takeaway
The objection confuses the created act with the acquired act; the verse's attribution to humans is via acquisition, not creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you truly believe that Allah created that act, or do you secretly think it was entirely your own doing? How would your repentance change if you fully internalized that you only acquired what Allah created?
?
Think of a good deed you are proud of. Can you sincerely attribute its success entirely to Allah's creation, or does your heart claim some of the credit? What would it mean to thank Allah for creating the action in you?
?
If every action of yours is created by Allah, are you willing to accept full responsibility for your acquisitions? Does this lesson make you feel more accountable or less? Be honest with yourself.
