The Objection on Human Agency: Can the Servant Be a Creator?
Verses, objection, and refutation on human agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that distinguishes human origination from divine creation, and the author's refutation that equates them based on the definition of khalq.

Core Idea
Creation is origination with estimation—and the servant's acts fit that definition.

The debate over human agency often turns on whether we truly create our actions. The objector argues that divine creation requires flawless precision, which human acts lack, so we are only 'originators,' not creators. The author responds by redefining creation simply as 'origination with estimation,' making human acts eligible for the label. This lesson walks through the verses, the objection, and the counter-argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic citations and their context
The author begins by quoting several verses that affirm God as the sole creator. Note how they establish divine prerogative over creation and command.
These verses set the stage for the objection.
2
Understand the objection's distinction
The objector concedes that the servant originates (mujid) his actions but denies he is a creator (khaliq), because creation implies precise estimation free from defect—something human actions lack.
The distinction hinges on perfection in estimation.
3
Analyze the author's answer
The author collapses the distinction by defining khalq simply as origination with estimation (ijad bi-taqdeer). He points out that human acts also conform to a specific measure, so if the servant truly originates his act, he would be a creator.
This refutes the objector by showing the definition does not require perfection.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider the theological consequence: if human acts are considered creation, does that undermine divine sovereignty? This point leads to deeper discussions in kalam about kasb (acquisition).
The author's argument opens the door for a nuanced view of human agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن هذا القبيل قوله تعالى ألا له الخلق والأمر خلق لكم ما في الأرض جميعاً وما خلقنا السماء والأرض وما بينهما باطلا وربنا الذي أعطى كل شيء خلقه ثم هدى . فإن قيل : على الوجوه نحن نجعل العبد موجداً لأفعاله، لا خالقاً لأن الخلق هو الإيجاد على وجه التقدير العاري عن الخلل. وعلى الوجه الذي يقدره وإيجاد العبد بر بما يقع على وجه الخلل، وعلى خلاف ما قدره . وجه (ه) قلنا : ليس الخلق إلا إيجاداً على وجه التقدير أي الإيقاع على قدر مخصوص، وفعل العبد ربما يكون كذلك. فلو كان هو موجداً له لكان خالصاً.
And of this kind is His statement, the Exalted: 'Surely, to Him belongs the creation and the command' (Quran 7:54), 'He created for you all that is on the earth' (Quran 2:29), 'And We did not create the heaven and the earth and what is between them in vain' (Quran 38:27), 'And our Lord who gave everything its creation, then guided' (Quran 20:50). Then it is said: According to the aspects, we make the servant the originator (mujid) of his actions, not a creator (khaliq), because creation (khalq) is origination (ijaad) with precise estimation (taqdeer) free from defect. And according to the aspect he estimates, the origination of the servant falls with defect and contrary to what he estimated. Aspect (h): We said: Creation is nothing but origination with estimation, i.e., bringing into existence upon a specific measure, and the servant's act may also be like that. So if he is its originator, he would be a creator (khaliq).
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical unit: the author first cites Quranic verses supporting that God is the sole creator, then presents an objection, and finally gives an answer. The objector argues that these verses only show the servant is the 'mujid' (originator) of his actions, not the 'khaliq' (creator), because 'khalq' implies precise estimation without any flaw, whereas human actions involve defects and deviations from intended plans. Thus, the servant cannot be called a creator. The author's answer (labeled 'wajh hā') redefines 'khalq' simply as 'ijaad with taqdeer' (origination with estimation, i.e., bringing into existence on a specific measured pattern). He points out that the servant's act may also conform to a specific measure, so if the servant truly originates his act, he would fit the definition of 'khaliq' (creator). This collapses the objector's distinction by showing that 'khalq' does not require perfection; it only requires origination with some estimation, which human acts can have.

Key Terms
خلق
إيجاد
تقدير
العبد موجد
خالص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because human actions are imperfect, they cannot be considered 'creation' in any sense.
Correction: The author shows that the definition of khalq does not require absolute perfection; it only requires origination with some estimation, which human acts can possess.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, why should the servant not be called a creator (khaliq) of his actions?
A) Because the servant does not originate his actions at all.
B) Because creation requires precise estimation free from defect, which human actions lack.
C) Because the Quran explicitly denies human creation.
D) Because the term khalq is exclusive to God by linguistic convention.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the objection by showing that creation (khalq) is simply origination with estimation, which applies to human acts if truly originated, thereby collapsing the distinction between human origination and divine creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that creation is simply origination with estimation. Think of an action you performed today that you carefully planned—say, preparing a meal or driving a route. Did it go exactly as you estimated? If it did, does that mean you, in a sense, 'created' that action? If it didn't, does that mean it was not truly your action? How does your answer shape your understanding of God's sole creatorship? Write down one area where your estimation was perfect, and one where it was not, and consider the implications for your responsibility before God.
?
The objector claims that human acts are flawed and thus cannot be considered creation. But the author replies that creation merely requires some specific measure, not perfection. Reflect on a persistent sin or failure in your life—something you intended to do perfectly but fell short. Does that mean God created that sin? Or do you own it? Let this passage press you to examine whether you truly believe your actions are your own, and what that means for your need for repentance and reliance on God's mercy.
?
This passage challenges you to define 'khalq' precisely. If you had to explain to a friend the difference between God creating the universe and a human building a house, how would you use the terms ' origination' and 'estimation'? What personal accountability does this definition demand from you when you speak of your own accomplishments? Are you tempted to take credit as if you were the ultimate creator? Spend a minute in silence, asking God to show you where your pride in your own 'creation' has obscured His sole creatorship.

The Fourth Proof: Divine Creation of Human Acts from the Verb Ja'ala
Proof using the verb ja'ala meaning transformation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verb ja'ala with two objects demonstrates that Allah creates human acts.

Core Idea
When 'ja'ala' transforms, Allah creates.

This lesson introduces the fourth proof for divine creation of human acts. The author uses Quranic verses where the verb 'ja'ala' (to make) takes two objects, indicating transformation. This linguistic argument shows that human actions are directly brought about by Allah, countering the Mu'tazila metaphorical interpretations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: a proof statement, Quranic examples, linguistic explanation, and rebuttal.
The passage is concise but packed with meaning.
2
Understand the verb ja'ala
Focus on the author's claim that ja'ala with two objects means transformation (tasyir). How does this apply to human acts?
This is the core of the proof.
3
Contrast with Mu'tazila
Note the Mu'tazila interpretation as metaphorical. Why does the author reject it?
The author argues the verses are too abundant and clear for forced interpretations.
4
Reflect on the strength
Consider the author's argument that such verses are too numerous and clear for forced interpretations.
This strengthens the Sunni position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليل الرابع قال ومنها نحو قوله تعالى حكاية ربنا واجعلنا مسلمين لك . رب اجعلني مقيم الصلاة . واجعله رب رضيا . فإن جعل المتعدي إلى مفعولين يكون بمعنى التصيير أي تحصيل صنعة مكان صنعة ، فإذا وقع مفعوله الثاني من أفعال العباد أفاد أنها بجعل الله وبخلقه، والمعتزلة يجعلون أمثال هذا مجازاً عن التوفيق، ومنح الألطاف والخذلان، ومنعها، أو التمكين والأقدار ونحو ذلك ، إلا أنها من الكثره والوضوح بحيث لا مجال لهذه التأويلات عند المنصف.
The fourth proof. He said: And among them is like His statement, the Exalted, narrating: 'Our Lord, make us submissive to You' (Quran 2:128), 'My Lord, make me an establisher of prayer' (Quran 14:40), 'And make him, my Lord, pleasing' (Quran 19:6). For the verb 'ja'ala' (made) when transitive to two objects has the meaning of transformation (tasyir), i.e., obtaining one condition in place of another. So when its second object falls among the actions of servants, it indicates that they are by God's making (ja'al) and His creation (khalq). The Mu'tazila consider such instances as metaphorical for divine guidance, granting of favors, abandonment (khidhlan), prevention, or enablement (tamkin) and destinies (aqdar) and the like. But they are of such abundance and clarity that there is no room for these interpretations for the fair-minded.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof for divine creation of human acts. The author cites Quranic verses where the verb 'ja'ala' (to make) is used with two objects, e.g., 'make us submissive', 'make me an establisher of prayer', 'make him pleasing'. He explains that 'ja'ala' in such constructions means 'tasyir' (transformation), i.e., to bring about one state in place of another. When the second object (the state) is a human act, the verse implies that God is the one who brings that act about—it is 'by God's making and creation.' The Mu'tazila reinterpret these verses metaphorically, claiming they refer to God's guidance, granting of grace, abandonment, prevention, or enabling of power. The author argues that such verses are too numerous and clear to admit these forced interpretations. The proof is thus a straightforward linguistic argument based on the standard meaning of 'ja'ala' with two objects.

Key Terms
جعل
تصيير
مفعول
المعتزلة
مجاز
توفيق
الخذلان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One may think that the verb 'ja'ala' merely means to designate or name something.
Correction: The author specifies that when 'ja'ala' takes two objects, it means transformation (tasyir), which involves directly effecting the state described in the second object.

Checkpoint
What is the key linguistic point the author uses in the fourth proof?
A) The verb 'ja'ala' always means 'to create'.
B) When 'ja'ala' has two objects, it means transformation, so the second object is a human act brought about by God.
C) The word 'ja'ala' is never used metaphorically in the Quran.
D) The verses are only about the Prophets, not ordinary people.

Key Takeaway
The verb ja'ala with two objects in the Quran indicates that Allah brings about human acts, refuting the Mu'tazila metaphorical interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah creates even your most personal acts of obedience (like establishing prayer), how does this change your sense of dependence on Him in your daily worship? Do you truly rely on Him to make you a person of prayer, or do you think it is solely your effort? What would change if you started each act of obedience with the awareness that it is Allah who brings it about?
?
The Mu'tazila reinterpreted clear verses to preserve human autonomy. Are there areas in your life where you reinterpret clear commands or truths from the Quran to preserve your own control or comfort? How does this lesson call you to submit to the apparent meaning of the Quran, even when it challenges your sense of self-sufficiency?
?
Think of a recent good deed you performed. Do you see it as a result of Allah's making (ja'ala) or as your own achievement? How does your answer affect your gratitude and humility? What step can you take today to acknowledge that every righteous action is ultimately from Allah?

The Sixth Proof: Verses of Divine Causation
Sanusi cites multiple Quranic verses and begins to explain their collective import.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses a collection of Quranic verses with diverse styles to construct the sixth proof that Allah is the sole creator of all actions and events.

Core Idea
Multiple Quranic verses, in different styles, all affirm Allah's exclusive causality.

In this lesson, we examine the sixth proof from the text. The author gathers a diverse set of Quranic verses that collectively demonstrate that Allah alone is the originator of all things—from blessings to creation, faith, emotions, travel, and natural phenomena. He then notes that despite their different styles, all these verses convey the same fundamental truth. We will explore these verses and understand how they form a cumulative proof that cannot be ignored.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of verses
Read through the Arabic passage and its translation. Note each verse and its reference. Ask yourself: what do these verses have in common?
The verses address different aspects of life—blessings, creation, faith, emotions, travel, and nature.
2
Understand the author's claim
The author says these verses are 'of different styles' but all convey the same desired meaning. What is that meaning? (Answer: that everything comes from Allah and He is the sole cause.)
The diversity of styles makes the argument stronger, as it shows the truth is expressed in multiple ways.
3
Consider the incompleteness
The passage breaks off mid-sentence. Think about what the author might be about to say next. He is likely beginning to comment on the apparent meaning of one specific verse to counter potential misinterpretations.
The lesson is based on the available text; the full argument is truncated.

Source Text

الدليل السادس قال (ومنها) قُل كل من عند الله . وما بكم من نعمة فمن الله . و إنما قولنا لشيء إذا أردناه أن نقول له كن فيكون . كتب في قلوبهم الإيمان . وأنه هو أضحك وأبكى . وهو الذي يسيركم في البر والبحر . وما يمسكهن إلا الله . إلى غير ذلك . هذه آيات مختلفة الأساليب في إفادة المطلوب، فالظاهر من قوله تعالى إن
The sixth proof. He said: And among them: 'Say: Everything is from Allah' (Quran 4:78), 'And whatever of blessings you have is from Allah' (Quran 16:53), 'Our only word to a thing when We will it is that We say to it 'Be', and it is' (Quran 16:40), 'He wrote faith in their hearts' (Quran 58:22), 'And that He is the One who makes laugh and makes weep' (Quran 53:43), 'And He is the One who enables you to travel through land and sea' (Quran 10:22), 'And none can hold them back except Allah' (Quran 16:79), and so on. These are verses of different styles in conveying the desired meaning. The apparent (zahir) from His statement, the Exalted, 'Indeed...'
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof, which is a collection of various Quranic verses that all point to God's sole causality over all things. The verses mention that everything is from Allah, all blessings are from Him, the creation of things by His command 'Be', God writing faith in hearts, causing laughter and weeping, enabling travel, and controlling the holding of heavenly bodies. The author notes that the styles of these verses are diverse, yet all serve the same purpose: affirming that God is the creator of all actions and events. The passage then begins to comment on the apparent meaning of one of these verses but is cut off at the page break. The incompleteness means the full argument is not presented here, but the pattern is clear: the multiplicity and clarity of these verses, just like in the previous proofs, leave no room for allegorical interpretation by the Mu'tazila.

Hadiths on God's Control Over Hearts
The author cites hadiths affirming that hearts are between God's fingers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that authentic hadiths affirm God's absolute control over human hearts, while still preserving human responsibility.

Core Idea
Hearts are between God's fingers—He turns them as He wills.

The author presents several hadiths that establish a core Islamic belief: Allah has complete sovereignty over the inclinations and states of the heart. These narrations show that even the Prophet himself sought divine assistance in keeping his heart firm on the religion. Let us examine the texts and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic hadiths and their translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to understand the meaning.
Notice the repetition of 'between two fingers of the Most Merciful'.
2
Identify the key phrase and its significance
The phrase 'between two fingers of the Most Merciful' is a metaphor for complete control. Consider what this implies about God's power over human hearts.
3
Reflect on the Prophet's supplication
The Prophet often prayed 'O Turner of hearts, make my heart firm upon Your religion.' Why would he ask this if he were not in need?
This shows that even the best of creation relies on God.
4
Understand the Prophet's illustration
When asked if he feared for his companions, the Prophet demonstrated by moving his fingers, showing how God turns hearts. This reinforces the lesson.
The physical gesture made the point tangible.
5
Appreciate the abundance of authentic hadiths
The author concludes that many authentic hadiths exist on this topic, indicating its importance in Islamic belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها قوله عليه السلام : «ما من قلب إلا وهو بين أصبعين من أصابع الرحمن إن شاء أن يقيمه أقامه وإن شاء أن يزيغه أزاغه) (۱) . وعن جابر رضي الله تعالى عنه : كان النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم كثيراً ما يقول يا مقـلـب القلوب ثبت قلبي على دينك» (۲) فقيل له : يا رسول الله أتخاف علينا وقد آمنا بك وبما حدثت به فقال إن القلوب بين اصبعين من أصابع الرحمن يقلبها هكذا، وأشار إلى السبابة والوسطى يحركها . (۳) والأحاديث الصحيحة في هذا الباب كثيرة .
And among them is his saying, peace be upon him: 'There is no heart except that it is between two fingers of the Most Merciful; if He wills to establish it, He establishes it, and if He wills to make it deviate, He makes it deviate.' (1) And from Jabir, may Allah be pleased with him: The Prophet, peace be upon him, used to say often: 'O Turner of hearts, make my heart firm upon Your religion.' (2) So it was said to him: O Messenger of Allah, do you fear for us while we have believed in you and in what you have brought? He said: 'Indeed, hearts are between two fingers of the Most Merciful; He turns them like this,' and he pointed with his forefinger and middle finger, moving them. (3) And the authentic hadiths on this topic are many.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage presents a collection of hadiths that emphasize divine sovereignty over human hearts. The first hadith states that every heart is between two fingers of the Most Merciful, meaning He has complete control to establish it or deviate it. The second hadith quotes the Prophet's frequent supplication to the Turner of hearts to keep his heart firm on the religion, indicating that even the Prophet seeks divine assistance. When asked if he fears for his companions, he confirms that hearts are between God's fingers, and he illustrates by moving his fingers. The passage concludes that authentic hadiths on this topic are abundant.

Key Terms

أصابع الرحمن
مقلب القلوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These hadiths negate human responsibility because God controls hearts completely.
Correction: The hadiths affirm divine control over the heart's inclinations, but they do not negate human responsibility. Believers are still commanded to act, pray, and strive, as the Prophet himself prayed for steadfastness while also teaching and guiding.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Prophet illustrated divine control by moving his fingers. Imagine his gesture before you. How does that visual affect your sense of reliance on Allah for the state of your own heart?

Refuting the Mu'tazilite Claim of Necessity
Sanusi presents and refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that human action is created by the agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite claim that human creation of actions is known by necessity is invalid.

Core Idea
Everyday experience proves voluntary action, not creation ex nihilo.

The Mu'tazila argue that it is self-evident that humans create their own actions, citing everyday distinctions like praising the good and blaming the bad. In this passage, Sanusi presents their argument and then exposes its flaw: it only shows that actions are voluntary, not that they are created by the agent. The real dispute is about creation, which requires a different proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite argument
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning to the point where they list their examples. Note their claim of necessity.
They use examples like requesting from the healthy but not the paralyzed.
2
Identify the response
Read Sanusi's response: the examples only prove voluntary action, not creation. The point of contention is creation, and most rational people reject the Mu'tazilite claim.
Notice the phrase 'المتنازع كونه بخلقه وإيجاده'.
3
Understand the refutation of necessity
Sanusi argues that claiming necessity is impudent, especially from skilled scholars. This would be deception.
This shows the weakness of their argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وأما المعتزلة)
أدلة المعتزلة على أن أفعال العباد واقعة بخلقهم وإيجادهم
منهم من ادعى الضرورة لأن كل أحد يفرق بين حركة سقوطه وصعوده، ويجد تصرفاته بحسب دواعيه ومقصوده، وبحكم مدح من أحسن وذم من أساء، وصحة طلب الشيء من الصحيح دون المقعد وصحة تحريك المدرة دون الجبل ولا شك في أن ما يطلبه أو ينهى عنه أو ما يتمناه إذ يتعجب منه، إنما هو فعل فاعله، كل ذلك بلا نظر وتأمل. والجواب : أنها لا تفيد سوى أن من الأفعال ما هو متعلق بقدرته وإرادته واقع بحسب قصده وداعيته والمتنازع كونه بخلقه وإيجاده، وقد خالف فيه أكثر العقلاء، فادعاء كونه ضرورياً آية الوقاحة فضرورة عمن هو في غاية الحذاقة لا يكون إلا تغبية وتلبيساً على أصحابه كيلا يتبين لهم رجوعه إلى الحق حيث ذهب إلى أن توقف ترجح القادر أحد طرفي الفعل والترك على الداعي ضروري، وحصول الفعل عقيب الداعي واجب أو ينتفي حينئذ استقلال العبد بالفاعلية، ويبطل الاعتزال بالكلية حيث لا يبقى المأمور مع الداعي الذي هو بخلق الله متمكناً من الفعل والترك، كما إذا كان نفس الفعل كذلك
قيل : المراد بالوجوب أنا نعلم أنه يفعل البتة مع إمكان الترك كثواب الأنبياء بالجنة، وعقاب الكفار بالنار. قلنا: إن لزم مع خلوص الداعي إيثار جانب الفعل بحيث لا يتمكن من الترك فذاك، وإلا فلا فالوجوب مجرد تسمية واعتقاد الحصول رجم بالغيب، وإنما يكون علماً إذا اعتقد وجوب الصدور، ولهذا يستدل بنفي الفعل على نفي القدرة والإرادة .
He said: (As for the Mu'tazila) The proofs of the Mu'tazila that the actions of servants occur by their creation and bringing into existence: Some of them claimed necessity because every person distinguishes between the movement of his falling and his ascending, and finds his actions according to his motives and intentions, and by the judgment of praising the good and blaming the bad, and the validity of requesting something from the healthy but not the paralyzed, and the validity of moving a pebble but not a mountain, and there is no doubt that what he requests or forbids or desires when he marvels at it is only the action of its doer, all of that without reflection and consideration. The response: That only indicates that among actions there is what is related to his power and will, occurring according to his intent and motive, but the point of dispute is whether it is by his creation and bringing into existence, and most rational people have disagreed with that. So claiming that it is necessary is a sign of impudence, for a necessity from someone who is extremely skilled is only stupidity and deception for his followers so that they do not realize his return to the truth, since he went to the view that the dependence of the capable agent's preponderance of one side of action and omission on the motive is necessary, and the occurrence of the action after the motive is obligatory, or else the servant's independence in agency is negated, and Mu'tazilism is invalidated entirely, because then the commanded person, with the motive that is created by God, is not able to choose action and omission, just as if the action itself were so. It was said: The intended meaning of obligation is that we know that he will definitely act while it is possible for him to refrain, like the reward of the prophets in Paradise and the punishment of the disbelievers in Hellfire. We said: If it entails with the pure motive preferring the side of action such that he cannot refrain, then that is the case; otherwise, the obligation is merely a name and a belief in occurrence without evidence, and it is only knowledge if one believes in the necessity of occurrence. For this reason, the absence of the action is used to infer the absence of power and will.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage begins by stating the Mu'tazilite position that human actions occur by their own creation and bringing into existence. Some of them claim this is known by necessity, arguing from everyday experience: people distinguish between falling and climbing, find their actions based on motives, receive praise and blame, can request movement from the healthy but not the paralyzed, and can move a pebble but not a mountain. They conclude that what is requested or prohibited is the action of the doer, without reflection. The response is that these observations only prove that some actions are related to a person's power and will, occurring according to his intent and motive. The real dispute is about creation and bringing into existence, and most rational people disagree with the Mu'tazilite claim. Therefore, claiming necessity is impudent; such a claim from a skilled person is only deception. The author then argues that the Mu'tazilite's own position—that the agent's preponderance depends on the motive as necessary and action follows the motive obligatorily—leads to negating the servant's independence and invalidates Mu'tazilism. If the motive created by God makes action necessary, then the servant cannot truly choose. An objection is raised: by 'obligatory' they mean we know he will definitely act while retaining the possibility of refraining, like rewards and punishments. The reply: if a pure motive necessarily leads to action without ability to refrain, then that is the case; otherwise, 'obligation' is just a name and belief without evidence. True knowledge requires belief in the necessity of occurrence, which is why the absence of action is taken as evidence of the absence of power and will.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
أفعال العباد
خلق
إيجاد
الضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilites think that because we experience voluntary action, we must be the creators of that action.
Correction: Voluntary action means we act by choice, but creation (bringing into existence) is a divine attribute. Experience only shows that we are agents in the sense of choosing, not that we create the action from nothing.

Checkpoint
What is the author's response to the Mu'tazilite claim that human creation of actions is known by necessity?
A) He accepts it as a valid proof.
B) He argues it only proves voluntary action, not creation.
C) He says it is a matter of consensus among Muslims.
D) He claims the Mu'tazilites are liars.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite claim of necessity is invalid because everyday experience only demonstrates voluntary agency, not creation ex nihilo.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You experience making decisions and moving your body. Do you ever feel that you are the ultimate cause of those actions? How does this passage challenge that feeling?
?
When you praise or blame yourself for a mistake, do you assume you could have done otherwise? Based on this lesson, does that assumption prove you created the action or only that you chose it?
?
The Mu'tazilites claimed their view was obvious. Are there beliefs you hold that you think are 'obvious' but have not examined? What would it cost you to question them in light of God's sole creative power?

Divine Decree
advanced
6 min
The Argument from Motive and Its Consequences
Sanusi presents a deeper refutation based on the Mu'tazilite theory of motive and obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's argument that the Mu'tazilite position on the motive leads to the negation of human free will and invalidates their own doctrine.

Core Idea
If action follows motive necessarily, then human free will is an illusion.

Having refuted the claim of necessity, Sanusi now turns to a deeper inconsistency in the Mu'tazilite position. He argues that if the motive makes action obligatory, then the servant cannot truly choose, which undermines the very basis of human accountability and the Mu'tazilite system. He then addresses an objection that tries to soften this by defining obligation as certainty of occurrence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite view on motive
Read how Sanusi characterizes the Mu'tazilite position: the agent's preponderance depends on motive as necessary, and action follows motive obligatorily.
Note the terms 'الداعي' and 'الوجوب'.
2
Follow the consequence
Sanusi argues that if action is necessary after a pure motive, then the servant is not an independent agent. This invalidates Mu'tazilism because then the commanded person is not truly able to choose.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Examine the objection and reply
An objection says 'obligatory' only means we know he will act, not that he cannot refrain. Sanusi responds: if the motive makes him unable to refrain, then it is true necessity; otherwise, it is just a belief without knowledge.
The reply shows that true knowledge requires certainty of occurrence.

Source Text

Checkpoint
What is the consequence Sanusi draws from the Mu'tazilite claim that action occurs obligatorily after a motive?
A) It confirms human free will.
B) It negates the servant's independence and invalidates Mu'tazilism.
C) It proves that actions are created by God.
D) It shows the necessity of divine grace.

Key Takeaway
If the motive compels action, human agency is an illusion; the Mu'tazilite position collapses into determinism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you genuinely feel that you chose it, or do you find yourself thinking 'God created the motive so it wasn't really my fault'? How does Sanusi's argument expose the danger of using divine decree as an excuse?
?
If every action you take is ultimately compelled by a motive that God creates, can you truly repent with sincerity? What does it mean for your relationship with God if you see yourself as a passive recipient rather than a responsible agent?
?
After studying this lesson, are you willing to fully accept that you are accountable for every choice—even the ones that felt inevitable? How will you begin to reclaim ownership of your actions in your daily prayers and intentions?

6 min
Mu'tazilite Claim of Necessity and Its Refutation
Al-Taftazani reports the Mu'tazilite claim of necessity and refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that human acts are necessarily self-created and al-Taftazani's refutation showing that it only proves choice, not creation.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilite 'necessity' proves only choice and motive, not creation.

In this passage, al-Taftazani presents and dismantles a central Mu'tazilite argument for human self-creation of actions. The Mu'tazilites claim that the human sense of distinction between voluntary and involuntary actions and the experience of acting according to motives make it necessarily known that humans create their own acts. Al-Taftazani refutes this by showing that these observations only indicate that actions are connected to the agent's power and will, not that they are created by the agent. He further exposes the logical problems in the Mu'tazilite position regarding the role of the motive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim
Read the first part of the passage where the Mu'tazilite argument is reported. Note the examples they use and the conclusion they draw.
The Mu'tazilites claim their conclusion is necessary (ḍarūrī).
2
Understand the refutation's core
Focus on the answer (الجواب) where al-Taftazani states what the observations actually prove and why the claim of necessity is rejected.
3
Analyze the logical problem of motive
Examine the deeper argument about the motive (داعي) and how it undermines Mu'tazilite independence.
Al-Taftazani argues that if the motive compels action, then the servant is not truly independent.
4
Consider the objection and reply
Look at the objection about 'necessity' meaning certainty of occurrence and al-Taftazani's final response.
Al-Taftazani distinguishes between genuine necessity and mere belief about future occurrence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وأما المعتزلة) أدلة المعتزلة على أن أفعال العباد واقعة بخلقهم وإيجادهم منهم من ادعى الضرورة لأن كل أحد يفرق بين حركة سقوطه وصعوده، ويجد تصرفاته بحسب دواعيه ومقصوده، وبحكم مدح من أحسن وذم من أساء، وصحة طلب الشيء من الصحيح دون المقعد وصحة تحريك المدرة دون الجبل ولا شك في أن ما يطلبه أو ينهى عنه أو ما يتمناه إذ يتعجب منه، إنما هو فعل فاعله، كل ذلك بلا نظر وتأمل. والجواب : أنها لا تفيد سوى أن من الأفعال ما هو متعلق بقدرته وإرادته واقع بحسب قصده وداعيته والمتنازع كونه بخلقه وإيجاده، وقد خالف فيه أكثر العقلاء، فادعاء كونه ضرورياً آية الوقاحة فضرورة عمن هو في غاية الحذاقة لا يكون إلا تغبية وتلبيساً على أصحابه كيلا يتبين لهم رجوعه إلى الحق حيث ذهب إلى أن توقف ترجح القادر أحد طرفي الفعل والترك على الداعي ضروري، وحصول الفعل عقيب الداعي واجب أو ينتفي حينئذ استقلال العبد بالفاعلية، ويبطل الاعتزال بالكلية حيث لا يبقى المأمور مع الداعي الذي هو بخلق الله متمكناً من الفعل والترك، كما إذا كان نفس الفعل كذلك قيل : المراد بالوجوب أنا نعلم أنه يفعل البتة مع إمكان الترك كثواب الأنبياء بالجنة، وعقاب الكفار بالنار. قلنا: إن لزم مع خلوص الداعي إيثار جانب الفعل بحيث لا يتمكن من الترك فذاك، وإلا فلا فالوجوب مجرد تسمية واعتقاد الحصول رجم بالغيب، وإنما يكون علماً إذا اعتقد وجوب الصدور، ولهذا يستدل بنفي الفعل على نفي القدرة والإرادة .
He said: (As for the Mu'tazilites) The proofs of the Mu'tazilites that the acts of servants occur by their creation and production: among them are those who claimed necessity because every person distinguishes between the movement of his falling and his rising, and finds his actions according to his motives and intentions, and judges the praise of one who does good and the blame of one who does evil, and the validity of requesting something from the healthy person but not the paralyzed, and the validity of moving a pebble but not a mountain, and there is no doubt that what he requests or prohibits or wishes for when amazed by it is only the act of its doer, all of that without reflection and deliberation. The answer: They only indicate that some acts are connected to his power and will, occurring according to his intention and motive; but the disputed point is whether it is by his creation and production, and most rational people have opposed it. So claiming it as necessary is a sign of impudence. For a necessity from someone who is extremely clever is nothing but delusion and deceit towards his followers so that they do not see his return to the truth, since he went to the position that the contingent's preferring one of the two sides of action and refraining depends on the motive is necessary, and the occurrence of the act consequent to the motive is necessary, otherwise the servant's independence in agency would be negated, and Mu'tazilism would be completely invalidated because the commanded person, with the motive which is created by God, would not be able to act or refrain, as if the act itself were likewise. It was said: The intended meaning of necessity is that we know that he will definitely act despite the possibility of refraining, like the reward of the prophets with Paradise and the punishment of the disbelievers with Hellfire. We said: If with a pure motive the preference for the side of action occurs such that he cannot refrain, then that is the case; otherwise, necessity is merely a name and belief in occurrence is shooting in the dark, and it is only knowledge if one believes the necessity of occurrence, and therefore one infers from the negation of the act the negation of power and will.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage presents a claim of the Mu'tazilites and its refutation. The Mu'tazilites assert that human acts are created by humans themselves, and some claim this is a necessary (ḍarūra) truth. They support it with several observations: people distinguish between voluntary and involuntary movements, they feel their actions align with motives, they praise and blame others, they can request actions from capable individuals, and they can move small objects but not large ones—all of which they say shows that the agent creates his own act without need for reflection. The answer (al-jawāb) refutes this by stating that these observations only prove that some actions are connected to the agent's power and will and occur according to his intention and motive. The real point of dispute is whether the act is created and produced by the agent. Since most rational people disagree with the Mu'tazilite claim, calling it necessary is a sign of impudence. The refutation then exposes a deeper problem: if the Mu'tazilite position were necessary, then a clever person's claim of necessity would be a trick to deceive his followers. In reality, the Mu'tazilite argument that the agent's preference for one side depends on the motive leads to the conclusion that the agent is not truly independent because the motive, which is created by God, would compel action, negating free choice. An objection is raised: by 'necessity' they might mean that we know the act will definitely occur despite the possibility of refraining, like the reward of prophets entering Paradise. The reply is that if the motive is so pure that the person cannot refrain, then that is genuine necessity; otherwise, it is just a name and an unfounded belief. True knowledge requires certainty of the necessity of occurrence. Thus, inference from the absence of an act to the absence of power and will is invalid.

Key Terms
ضرورة
داعي
فاعلية
وجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilite observations of voluntary action prove that humans necessarily create their own acts.
Correction: The observations only show that actions are connected to the agent's power and will, not that they are created by the agent.

Checkpoint
What is the core of al-Taftazani's refutation of the Mu'tazilite claim of necessity?
A) The Mu'tazilite arguments are contradictory.
B) The observations only prove that actions are connected to the agent's power and will, not that they are created by him.
C) The Mu'tazilites reject Divine power entirely.
D) The claim of necessity is based on revelation, not reason.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite claim of necessity for human self-creation is refuted because their observations only show that actions are connected to the agent's power and will, not that they are created by the agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel proud of a good deed, do you recognize that the very motive to do it was created by Allah? Can you claim any real ownership of your obedience without acknowledging His complete sovereignty over your will?
?
This lesson reveals that even the most 'obvious' feelings of self-agency can be a deception. What hidden pride or self-reliance in your heart might be blinding you from seeing that your actions are entirely dependent on Allah's creation—your motives included?
?
If you were to truly internalize that every action, even the choice to believe, is ultimately dependent on a motive Allah creates, how would that change your morning prayer? Would you still ask for guidance as if you have the power to choose it yourself?

Five Arguments of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri for Human Agency
Al-Taftazani enumerates five arguments by Abu al-Husayn al-Basri for human creation of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five rational arguments advanced by Abu al-Husayn al-Basri to prove that humans create their own actions, and the reasoning behind each argument.

Core Idea
Humans necessarily distinguish voluntary from involuntary acts, proving they create their own actions.

Al-Taftazani presents five arguments from Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, each grounded in necessary knowledge (ḍarūrī). These arguments aim to show that human actions are created by the humans themselves, not by God alone. The arguments range from the distinction between voluntary and involuntary movements to the necessity of praise and blame, and the knowledge of one's own power.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening classification
Read the first sentence noting the split into two groups and the claim that the judgment is necessary. This sets the context.
Abu al-Husayn's group claims the judgment is ingrained in fair-minded people.
2
Identify each of the five arguments
Go through each argument (first to fifth) and note the specific example given (e.g., walking vs. trembling, eating when hungry).
Each argument appeals to necessary knowledge (ḍarūrī).
3
Analyze the reasoning
For each argument, ask: How does this show that the human creates the action? Notice the logical structure (e.g., distinction, motive, praise/blame, ability).
The fourth argument uses a fortiori reasoning.
4
Consider the Ash'arite response
Read the common misunderstanding and reflect on how an Ash'arite might object that distinction only shows choice, not independent creation.
This connects the lesson to broader Aqeedah debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القائلون بأن أفعال العباد واقعة بخلقهم وإيجادهم استقلالاً، افترقوا فرقتين. فأبو الحسين البصري وأتباعه ، ادعوا أن هذا الحكم ضروري مكروز في عقول العقلاء المنصفين الخالين عن تقليد أسلافهم، وذكروا في ذلك وجوهاً على قصد التنبيه أو الاستدلال، فإنه ربما يكون الحكم ضرورياً، والحكم بضروريته استدلالياً. الأول : أن كل أحد يفرق بالضرورة بين حركاته الاختيارية كالمشي على الأرض والصعود إلى الجبل، والاضطرارية كالارتعاش والسقوط من السطح، وما ذاك إلا بأن الأولى بقدرته وإيجاده بخلاف الثانية . الثاني : أن كل أحد يعلم بالضرورة أن تصرفاته واقعة بحسب قصده وداعيته ، كالإقدام على الأكل والشرب عند اشتداد الجوع ، والإحجام عنهما إذا علم أن في الطعام والماء سماً، ولا معنى لوجود الفعل بالاختيار إلا الذي يحدث منه الفعل على وفق دواعيه . الثالث : أن كل فاعل يعلم بالضرورة حسن مدح من أحسن إليه وذم من أساء ، ولولا أنه يعلم بالضرورة كونه المحدث لتلك الأفعال لما حكم بذلك، كما لا يحكم بحسن المدح والذم على ما ليس من أفعاله، ولهذا إذا رمي العاقل ندم الرامي لا الآخرة . الرابع : أنه يعلم بالضرورة صحة القيام أو المشي من الصحيح البنية لا من الزمن والمقعد بناء على صحة حدوثها من الأول دون الثاني، وإذا كان الفرع ضرورياً فالأصل بطريق الأولى. الخامس : أنه يعلم بالضرورة أنه يصح منه تحريك المدرة دون الجبل، ولا معنى سوى العلم بقدرته على تحريكها دونه، ولهذا يقصد الحار طرف الجدول الضيق دون الواسع .
Those who say that the acts of servants occur by their creation and production independently split into two groups. Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and his followers claimed that this judgment is necessary, ingrained in the minds of fair-minded people free from the imitation of their forefathers. They mentioned concerning that some arguments aiming at reminding or inference, for perhaps the judgment is necessary, but the judgment of its necessity is inferential. The first: Every person necessarily distinguishes between his voluntary movements, like walking on the ground and climbing a mountain, and involuntary ones, like trembling and falling from a roof; and that is only because the former are by his power and creation unlike the latter. The second: Every person necessarily knows that his actions occur according to his intention and motive, such as proceeding to eat and drink when hunger is severe, and refraining from them if he knows there is poison in the food and water; and there is no meaning to the existence of an act by choice except that he brings about the act according to his motives. The third: Every agent necessarily knows the goodness of praising one who does good to him and blaming one who does evil; and were it not that he necessarily knows that he is the one who brings about those acts, he would not judge that, just as he does not judge the goodness of praise and blame for what is not his acts; and for this reason, if an arrow is shot, the archer is blamed, not the stone. The fourth: He necessarily knows the validity of standing or walking from the healthy-bodied person but not from the disabled and paralyzed, based on the validity of their occurrence from the first without the second; and if the corollary is necessary, the principle is a fortiori. The fifth: He necessarily knows that it is valid for him to move a pebble but not a mountain; and there is no meaning except the knowledge of his power to move it without him; and for this reason, the digger aims for the narrow side of the ditch, not the wide.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage presents five arguments (wujūh) from Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and his followers to prove that human acts occur by the human's own creation and production independently. The author first notes that those who hold this view split into two groups, and Abu al-Husayn's group claims the judgment is necessary (ḍarūrī) and ingrained in the minds of fair-minded people free from blind imitation. They offer these arguments either as reminders or as inferences, since sometimes the judgment itself is necessary but the judgment of its necessity is inferential. The first argument: People necessarily distinguish between voluntary movements (like walking and climbing) and involuntary ones (like trembling and falling). The distinction shows that voluntary movements are by the agent's own power and creation, unlike involuntary ones. The second argument: People necessarily know that their actions align with their intentions and motives—they eat when hungry and refrain when they fear poison. The very meaning of a voluntary act is that the agent brings it about according to his motives. The third argument: People necessarily judge that it is good to praise a benefactor and blame a malefactor. This judgment presupposes that the agent is the one who brought about the act, because one does not praise or blame for acts that are not one's own—the archer is blamed for the arrow, not the stone. The fourth argument: People necessarily know that actions like standing or walking are possible from a healthy person but not from a disabled person. Since the corollary (the possibility of action) is necessary, the principle (that the agent has power and creates the act) is even more necessary by a fortiori reasoning. The fifth argument: People necessarily know that they can move a small pebble but not a large mountain. This shows they have power to move the pebble but not the mountain; the very knowledge of their power indicates they are the creator of the act. The example of a digger choosing the narrow side of a ditch illustrates that the agent directs his act according to his ability.

Key Terms
ضروري
اختياري
اضطراري
داعية
مدح
ذم
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think these arguments prove that humans create their acts independently of God.
Correction: Ash'arite theologians would respond that the distinction between voluntary and involuntary only shows the agent's choice, not that the act is created by the agent without Divine power.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the third argument of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri?
A) The difference between voluntary and involuntary movements.
B) The correlation of actions with intentions and motives.
C) The judgment of praise and blame for actions.
D) The ability to move small objects but not large ones.

Key Takeaway
Abu al-Husayn al-Basri argues that five lines of necessary knowledge—from voluntary distinction to motive, praise, ability, and power—prove that humans create their own actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know you necessarily distinguish between voluntary and involuntary acts. In what specific area of your life are you pretending that a sin is involuntary—blaming your temper, your environment, or your past—when deep down you know you are the one choosing it? What will you do today to stop making excuses and take full ownership before Allah?
?
The archer is blamed, not the stone. When you sin, do you blame your nafs, shaytan, or circumstances instead of yourself? How can you truly become the archer who owns his arrow—starting with one sin you will confess and repent from today, with no justification?
?
If you truly believe you are the creator of your actions as this lesson argues, then your accountability before Allah is immense. What one action—good or bad—do you need to realize is entirely your creation, and how will you change your relationship with Allah because of that certainty, beginning now?

Divine Decree
intermediate
5 min
The Self-Evident Distinction Between Voluntary and Involuntary Actions
Abu al-Husayn presents the introductory claim and first argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's claim that the judgment of human agency is self-evident, and his first argument based on the necessary distinction between voluntary and involuntary movements.

Core Idea
We naturally know our voluntary actions are our own doing.

Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and his followers argue that rational people, free from blind tradition, necessarily know that humans independently create their actions. They present six arguments to alert or prove this necessary judgment. The first argument draws on the clear distinction everyone makes between voluntary movements (like walking) and involuntary ones (like trembling). This lesson unpacks that foundational argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage that includes the introductory claim and the first argument. Notice the phrase 'ضروري مكروز في عقول العقلاء'.
2
Understand the claim
Identify that Abu al-Husayn claims the judgment is necessary but demonstrating its necessity can be inferential.
This nuance avoids circular reasoning.
3
Analyze the first argument
Examine the distinction between voluntary and involuntary movements. Reflect on why this distinction points to human agency.
Notice the use of 'بالضرورة' (necessarily) in the argument.
4
Connect to lesson objective
See how this argument grounds the claim that humans are the creators of their voluntary acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القائلون بأن أفعال العباد واقعة بخلقهم وإيجادهم استقلالاً، افترقوا فرقتين. فأبو الحسين البصري وأتباعه، ادعوا أن هذا الحكم ضروري مكروز في عقول العقلاء المنصفين الخالين عن تقليد أسلافهم، وذكروا في ذلك وجوهاً على قصد التنبيه أو الاستدلال، فإنه ربما يكون الحكم ضرورياً، والحكم بضروريته استدلالياً.
الأول: أن كل أحد يفرق بالضرورة بين حركاته الاختيارية كالمشي على الأرض والصعود إلى الجبل، والاضطرارية كالارتعاش والسقوط من السطح، وما ذاك إلا بأن الأولى بقدرته وإيجاده بخلاف الثانية.
الثاني: أن كل أحد يعلم بالضرورة أن تصرفاته واقعة بحسب قصده وداعيته، كالإقدام على الأكل والشرب عند اشتداد الجوع، والإحجام عنهما إذا علم أن في الطعام والماء سماً، ولا معنى لوجود الفعل بالاختيار إلا الذي يحدث منه الفعل على وفق دواعيه.
الثالث: أن كل فاعل يعلم بالضرورة حسن مدح من أحسن إليه وذم من أساء، ولولا أنه يعلم بالضرورة كونه المحدث لتلك الأفعال لما حكم بذلك، كما لا يحكم بحسن المدح والذم على ما ليس من أفعاله، ولهذا إذا رمي العاقل ندم الرامي لا الآخرة.
الرابع: أنه يعلم بالضرورة صحة القيام أو المشي من الصحيح البنية لا من الزمن والمقعد بناء على صحة حدوثها من الأول دون الثاني، وإذا كان الفرع ضرورياً فالأصل بطريق الأولى.
الخامس: أنه يعلم بالضرورة أنه يصح منه تحريك المدرة دون الجبل، ولا معنى سوى العلم بقدرته على تحريكها دونه، ولهذا يقصد الحار طرف الجدول الضيق دون الواسع.
السادس: أن الطالب العاقل يعلم بالضرورة أنه يطلب ما يحدثه المأمور، ولهذا ما يتلطف في استدعاء ذلك الفعل منه، وأنه ينهى عما يكرهه من الأفعال التي يحدثها المنهي، وكذا التمني والتعجب وغير ذلك، وكل هذا يدل على أن فعل العبد إحداثه.
Those who say that the actions of servants occur by their own creation and bringing into existence independently divided into two groups. Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and his followers claimed that this judgment is necessary (daruri), ingrained in the minds of fair-minded rational people free from the imitation of their predecessors. They mentioned in that matter arguments (wujuh) for the purpose of alerting or inferring, for perhaps the judgment is necessary, but the judgment of its necessity is inferential (istidlali).
First: That everyone necessarily distinguishes between his voluntary movements, like walking on the ground and climbing a mountain, and involuntary ones like trembling and falling from a roof; and that is only because the first are by his power (qudra) and his bringing into existence, contrary to the second.
Second: That everyone necessarily knows that his actions occur according to his intention (qasd) and motive (da'iya), like proceeding to eat and drink when hunger intensifies, and refraining from them if he knows that the food and water contain poison; and there is no meaning to the existence of the voluntary act except that which he produces from himself in accordance with his motives.
Third: That every agent necessarily knows the goodness of praising the one who does good to him and blaming the one who does evil; and were it not that he necessarily knows that he is the one who brings about those actions, he would not judge that, just as he does not judge the goodness of praise and blame concerning that which is not his actions. For this reason, when a rational person shoots, the shooter regrets, not the other.
Fourth: That he necessarily knows the soundness of standing or walking from the healthy-bodied person, not from the paralyzed or crippled, based on the soundness of their occurrence from the first but not the second; and if the branch is necessary, then the root is all the more so.
Fifth: That he necessarily knows that it is possible for him to move the pebble but not the mountain; and there is no meaning other than the knowledge of his power to move it without that; and for this reason, the hot person aims for the narrow edge of the stream, not the wide one.
Sixth: That the rational petitioner necessarily knows that he requests what the commanded person brings about, and for this reason he is gentle in requesting that action from him, and that he forbids what he dislikes of the actions that the forbidden person brings about; likewise wishing, wondering, and other than that. And all of this indicates that the servant's action is his own bringing about.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage presents the six arguments (wujuh) of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and his followers, who claim that the judgment that humans independently create their own actions is necessary and self-evident to sound minds free from blind tradition. They first state that although the judgment itself may be necessary, demonstrating its necessity can be inferential. Then they list six arguments. The first argument distinguishes voluntary from involuntary movements, attributing the former to one's own power and creation. The second argument points to the knowledge that actions follow one's intention and motive, so voluntary action is that which one produces from oneself according to motives. The third argument uses the universal practice of praising benefactors and blaming malefactors, implying that we know we are the agents of those actions. The fourth argues that we necessarily know that certain actions proceed from a healthy person but not from a disabled one, so by analogy the root cause must be the person's capacity. The fifth argues that we know we have power over some actions (e.g., moving a pebble) but not over others (e.g., moving a mountain). The sixth argues that a request or prohibition is directed at what the person himself can bring about, and we see gentle requests and prohibitions, indicating that the servant is the one who brings about his own actions. The conclusion of each argument is that the servant independently creates his own act.

Key Terms
ضروري (daruri)
اختياري (ikhtiyari)
اضطرار (idtarar)
قدرة (qudra)
إيجاد (ijad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because we distinguish voluntary from involuntary actions, this proves we create them independently of God.
Correction: The author will later argue that this only proves voluntary action, not absolute creation; the Ash'ari position allows for acquisition (kash) where God creates the act but the human acquires it.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, why do we necessarily distinguish between voluntary and involuntary movements?
A) Because voluntary movements are smooth
B) Because voluntary movements are by our power and creation
C) Because involuntary movements are painful
D) Because we learn this from teachers

Key Takeaway
The natural distinction we all make between voluntary and involuntary actions suggests that we are the agents of our voluntary acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you honestly feel that it was entirely your own doing, or do you subconsciously attribute it to God's creation to lessen your responsibility? How would your repentance change if you fully owned the act?
?
Think of a recent moment when you chose to do something difficult (like waking for Fajr) and a moment you trembled with cold. Do you treat the first as truly your achievement and the second as beyond your control? Why is it easy to claim credit for the voluntary but shift blame for sins?
?
If the distinction between voluntary and involuntary is self-evident, what does that say about your daily excuses? Will you start today by acknowledging that every voluntary act is your creation and you are accountable for it?

Divine Decree
intermediate
5 min
Arguments from Intention, Motive, and Moral Responsiveness
Second and third arguments of Abu al-Husayn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu al-Husayn's second and third arguments, which rely on knowledge of intention/motive and the practice of praise and blame.

Core Idea
We know our actions follow our intentions, and we praise and blame accordingly.

Abu al-Husayn continues his set of self-evident proofs by pointing to two more universal human experiences: (1) everyone knows that their actions occur according to their intentions and motives, and (2) everyone naturally praises those who benefit them and blames those who harm them, implying we recognize those as our own acts. These arguments build on the first by showing that our sense of agency extends to purposive actions and moral responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument
Read the second argument carefully. Notice the examples of eating when hungry and refraining from poison. Ask yourself: why does this show the action is mine?
The key phrase is 'بقصده وداعيته' (by his intention and motive).
2
Read the third argument
Read the third argument. Consider the universal practice of praising benefactors and blaming wrongdoers. Why does this presuppose that the person is the agent?
The author says without that knowledge, we would not judge praise and blame.
3
Reflect on the logical connection
Both arguments rely on necessary knowledge. The second uses internal motivation, the third uses external moral responses. See how they converge on human agency.
4
Consider the implication
Think about how these arguments strengthen the claim that we independently create our actions.
But note the upcoming critique that these only show acquisition, not creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
According to the third argument, why do we praise someone who does good to us?
A) Because they performed an action good for us
B) Because we necessarily know they are the agent of that good action
C) Because society expects it
D) Because we want to be polite

Key Takeaway
Our knowledge of intention and our practices of praise and blame show that we recognize ourselves as agents of our voluntary actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent sin you committed. Did you, in that moment, truly see yourself as the one who brought it about, or did you mentally shift the cause to something else? How does the second argument—that your action follows your intention—challenge you to own that sin before Allah?
?
You know the goodness of praising the one who does good to you. But when did you last sincerely praise someone for their good deed toward you? And when did you last blame yourself for a wrong, rather than excusing it? What does your practice of praise and blame reveal about your belief in your own responsibility?
?
The shooter regrets his shot, not the target. When you face regret today, do you trace it back to your own choices, or do you blame circumstances, people, or even Allah's decree? What would it mean for your repentance if you fully accepted that you are the one who brought about your actions?

Divine Decree
intermediate

Arguments from Capacity, Ability, and Request
Fourth, fifth, and sixth arguments of Abu al-Husayn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu al-Husayn's fourth, fifth, and sixth arguments, which appeal to necessary knowledge about capacity, power, and the nature of requests and prohibitions.

Core Idea
We know our capacities and that requests are directed at what we can do.

Abu al-Husayn completes his series of arguments by pointing to three more self-evident truths. The fourth argues from the ability of a healthy person versus a disabled one to perform actions. The fifth points to our knowledge of which actions we have power over (moving a pebble vs. a mountain). The sixth notes that requests and prohibitions are directed at actions we can bring about. Together, these arguments further solidify the claim that humans are the creators of their own acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth argument
Read the argument about the healthy person vs. the disabled. Notice the logical structure: if the ability to stand is necessary for the healthy, then the cause of standing must be the person's capacity.
Key phrase: 'إذا كان الفرع ضرورياً فالأصل بطريق الأولى'.
2
Read the fifth argument
Read the argument about moving a pebble vs. a mountain. Reflect on how knowledge of one's own power is self-evident.
The example of the hot person reaching the narrow edge illustrates knowing one's capabilities.
3
Read the sixth argument
Read the last argument about the rational petitioner. Understand that requests and prohibitions imply the person can bring about the action.
Note the terms 'طلب', 'نهى', 'تمني', 'تعجب'.
4
Synthesize the six arguments
Consider how all six arguments converge on the same conclusion: the human is the independent creator of his voluntary actions. But also remember that Ash'aris will reinterpret this as acquisition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القائلون بأن أفعال العباد واقعة بخلقهم وإيجادهم استقلالاً، افترقوا فرقتين. فأبو الحسين البصري وأتباعه، ادعوا أن هذا الحكم ضروري مكروز في عقول العقلاء المنصفين الخالين عن تقليد أسلافهم، وذكروا في ذلك وجوهاً على قصد التنبيه أو الاستدلال، فإنه ربما يكون الحكم ضرورياً، والحكم بضروريته استدلالياً.
الأول: أن كل أحد يفرق بالضرورة بين حركاته الاختيارية كالمشي على الأرض والصعود إلى الجبل، والاضطرارية كالارتعاش والسقوط من السطح، وما ذاك إلا بأن الأولى بقدرته وإيجاده بخلاف الثانية.
الثاني: أن كل أحد يعلم بالضرورة أن تصرفاته واقعة بحسب قصده وداعيته، كالإقدام على الأكل والشرب عند اشتداد الجوع، والإحجام عنهما إذا علم أن في الطعام والماء سماً، ولا معنى لوجود الفعل بالاختيار إلا الذي يحدث منه الفعل على وفق دواعيه.
الثالث: أن كل فاعل يعلم بالضرورة حسن مدح من أحسن إليه وذم من أساء، ولولا أنه يعلم بالضرورة كونه المحدث لتلك الأفعال لما حكم بذلك، كما لا يحكم بحسن المدح والذم على ما ليس من أفعاله، ولهذا إذا رمي العاقل ندم الرامي لا الآخرة.
الرابع: أنه يعلم بالضرورة صحة القيام أو المشي من الصحيح البنية لا من الزمن والمقعد بناء على صحة حدوثها من الأول دون الثاني، وإذا كان الفرع ضرورياً فالأصل بطريق الأولى.
الخامس: أنه يعلم بالضرورة أنه يصح منه تحريك المدرة دون الجبل، ولا معنى سوى العلم بقدرته على تحريكها دونه، ولهذا يقصد الحار طرف الجدول الضيق دون الواسع.
السادس: أن الطالب العاقل يعلم بالضرورة أنه يطلب ما يحدثه المأمور، ولهذا ما يتلطف في استدعاء ذلك الفعل منه، وأنه ينهى عما يكرهه من الأفعال التي يحدثها المنهي، وكذا التمني والتعجب وغير ذلك، وكل هذا يدل على أن فعل العبد إحداثه.
Those who say that the actions of servants occur by their own creation and bringing into existence independently divided into two groups. Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and his followers claimed that this judgment is necessary (daruri), ingrained in the minds of fair-minded rational people free from the imitation of their predecessors. They mentioned in that matter arguments (wujuh) for the purpose of alerting or inferring, for perhaps the judgment is necessary, but the judgment of its necessity is inferential (istidlali).
First: That everyone necessarily distinguishes between his voluntary movements, like walking on the ground and climbing a mountain, and involuntary ones like trembling and falling from a roof; and that is only because the first are by his power (qudra) and his bringing into existence, contrary to the second.
Second: That everyone necessarily knows that his actions occur according to his intention (qasd) and motive (da'iya), like proceeding to eat and drink when hunger intensifies, and refraining from them if he knows that the food and water contain poison; and there is no meaning to the existence of the voluntary act except that which he produces from himself in accordance with his motives.
Third: That every agent necessarily knows the goodness of praising the one who does good to him and blaming the one who does evil; and were it not that he necessarily knows that he is the one who brings about those actions, he would not judge that, just as he does not judge the goodness of praise and blame concerning that which is not his actions. For this reason, when a rational person shoots, the shooter regrets, not the other.
Fourth: That he necessarily knows the soundness of standing or walking from the healthy-bodied person, not from the paralyzed or crippled, based on the soundness of their occurrence from the first but not the second; and if the branch is necessary, then the root is all the more so.
Fifth: That he necessarily knows that it is possible for him to move the pebble but not the mountain; and there is no meaning other than the knowledge of his power to move it without that; and for this reason, the hot person aims for the narrow edge of the stream, not the wide one.
Sixth: That the rational petitioner necessarily knows that he requests what the commanded person brings about, and for this reason he is gentle in requesting that action from him, and that he forbids what he dislikes of the actions that the forbidden person brings about; likewise wishing, wondering, and other than that. And all of this indicates that the servant's action is his own bringing about.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage presents the six arguments (wujuh) of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and his followers, who claim that the judgment that humans independently create their own actions is necessary and self-evident to sound minds free from blind tradition. They first state that although the judgment itself may be necessary, demonstrating its necessity can be inferential. Then they list six arguments. The first argument distinguishes voluntary from involuntary movements, attributing the former to one's own power and creation. The second argument points to the knowledge that actions follow one's intention and motive, so voluntary action is that which one produces from oneself according to motives. The third argument uses the universal practice of praising benefactors and blaming malefactors, implying that we know we are the agents of those actions. The fourth argues that we necessarily know that certain actions proceed from a healthy person but not from a disabled one, so by analogy the root cause must be the person's capacity. The fifth argues that we know we have power over some actions (e.g., moving a pebble) but not over others (e.g., moving a mountain). The sixth argues that a request or prohibition is directed at what the person himself can bring about, and we see gentle requests and prohibitions, indicating that the servant is the one who brings about his own actions. The conclusion of each argument is that the servant independently creates his own act.

Key Terms
قدرة (qudra)
إيجاد (ijad)
المحدث (al-muhdith)
كسب (kash)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the sixth argument, why does a rational petitioner use gentle language in requesting?
A) To be polite
B) Because he knows the requested action is up to the commanded person
C) Because he wants to influence the commanded person's will
D) Because the action is difficult

Key Takeaway
Our self-evident knowledge of capacities and the nature of requests confirm that the servant brings about his own actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
هل تعرف حقًا أنك قادر على فعل الطاعات والابتعاد عن المعاصي؟ إذا كنت تعلم ذلك بالضرورة، فلماذا تؤجل التوبة أو تتهاون في الواجبات؟ فكر في مثال من حياتك اليوم حيث استخدمت هذه المعرفة عن قدرتك في طاعة الله، وأين فرطت فيها.
?
عندما تدعو الله، هل تطلب منه أن يفعل شيئًا لك، أم أنك تدرك أنك أنت من يجب أن يفعله؟ استحضر موقفًا دعوت فيه الله بشيء ثم لم تبذل جهدك في تحقيقه. ما الذي يمنعك من الجمع بين الدعاء والعمل؟
?
تأمل في طلبك من الآخرين: لماذا تتلطف مع بعضهم؟ لأنك تعلم أن الفعل منهم. فكيف حال دعائك لله؟ هل تتعامل معه كأنه طلب من قادر مطلق، أم كأنه مجرد عادة؟ هل شعرت يومًا بالخجل من كثرة طلبك دون عمل؟

Divine Decree

8 min
The Sixth Proof: Command and Prohibition Imply Only Acquisition, Not Creation
The author presents the sixth argument and limits it to kasb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the rational evidence from command and prohibition only establishes human acquisition (kasb) of actions, not creation.

Core Idea
Command and prohibition prove human responsibility, not human creation of actions.

This lesson examines the sixth proof from the text, which deals with command and prohibition. The author argues that although the rational seeker knows by necessity that he requests actions from the commanded person, this only shows that the action is acquired by the human, not that the human creates it. We will see how the author distinguishes between kasb and creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then compare with the literal translation. Notice the flow from the initial claim to the author's correction.
The passage begins with a common argument and then refines it.
2
Identify the claim that command implies creation
The opening states the apparent implication: command/prohibition show that the servant originates the action. Understand that this is the view the author challenges.
3
Understand the author's limitation to kasb
The author explains that these evidences only show that actions are voluntary and acquired by the servant's power and will, but they are still created by God. This is the key distinction.
This refutes the notion that humans create their own actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : أن الطالب العاقل يعلم بالضرورة أنه يطلب ما يحدثه المأمور، ولهذا يتلطف في استدعاء ذلك الفعل منه، وأنه ينهى عما يكرهه من الأفعال التي يحدثها المنهي، وكذا التمني والتعجب وغير ذلك، وكل هذا يدل على أن فعل العبد إحداثه، والواجب أن هذه الوجوه لا تفيد سوى أن من الأفعال المستندة إلى العبد ما هو متعلق بقدرته وإرادته واقع بحسب قصده وداعيته وهي المسماة بالأفعال الاختيارية، وكونها مقدورة للعبد واقعة بكسبه، وعلى حسب قصده واختياره، وعند صرف قدرته وإرادته، وإن كانت مخلوقة لله تعالى كان في حسن المدح والذم وصحة الطلب والنهي والتمني والتعجب ونحو ذلك، ولا يفيد كونها مخلوقة للعبد على ما هو المتنازع، فضلاً أن تفيد العلم الضروري بذلك
The sixth: That the rational seeker knows necessarily that he seeks what the commanded one produces, and therefore he is gentle in requesting that action from him, and that he forbids what he dislikes of actions that the forbidden one produces, and likewise desire, wonder, etc. And all this indicates that the servant's action is his [own] bringing into existence. The obligatory [view] is that these aspects only yield that some actions attributed to the servant are those pertaining to his power and will, occurring according to his intention and motive, which are called voluntary actions, and that they are within the servant's power, occurring by his acquisition (kasb), and according to his intention and choice, and upon directing his power and will, even though they are created by God Most High, there is in that the goodness of praise and blame and the validity of command, prohibition, desire, wonder, etc. And it does not yield that they are created by the servant, which is the disputed point, let alone that it yields necessary knowledge of that.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is a definition and argument. The author states the sixth point: the rational seeker knows by necessity that he requests the action of the commanded person and prohibits disliked actions, and that indicates that the servant's action is his own creation. However, the author clarifies that these evidences only prove that some actions are voluntary and acquired (kasb) by the servant's power and will, but they are created by God. This does not prove that the servant creates his own action, which is the disputed point, let alone that it provides necessary knowledge of that.

Key Terms
ضرورة
كسب
اختياري
قدرة
إرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Command and prohibition prove that humans create their own actions independently of God.
Correction: They only prove that actions are acquired (kasb) by humans, not that they are created by humans. Creation remains exclusively with God.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what do the evidences from command, prohibition, desire, and wonder prove about human actions?
A) That humans create their own actions
B) That human actions are acquired (kasb) by them and occur by their power but are created by God
C) That human actions are solely caused by God with no human role
D) That human actions are impossible

Key Takeaway
Command and prohibition (and related emotions) only establish that humans acquire their actions voluntarily; they do not prove that humans create their actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself repeatedly falling into a sin, do you honestly attribute it to your own choice and acquisition, or do you passively feel that 'God created it' and thus excuse yourself? How does the doctrine of kasb force you to own your actions without despairing of God's mercy?
?
If you truly believed that your every action—good and bad—is acquired by you but created by God, would you still make the same excuses for your negligence? What would change in your daily prayers if you actually internalized that you are responsible for your kasb?
?
Consider the last time you praised someone or blamed someone. Did your heart recognize that their action was ultimately created by God, yet still worthy of praise or blame? How can you hold others accountable without forgetting that their actions, like yours, are only acquired by them?
